Why Only Specific Existence Needs a Creator
Proof that particular existence needs a cause while general existence does not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why general existence does not require a cause, while specific existence does, and how this relates to the Creator's knowledge and will.

Core Idea
General existence needs no cause; only particular existence depends on the Creator.

This passage clarifies a pivotal distinction in Islamic theology: existence in its absolute sense is not contingent, but every specific, determinate existence is. Before examining the proof, it is essential to grasp this conceptual difference, as it underpins the argument for a Creator who knows and wills each particular thing.

Guided Path
1
Read and get an overview
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the contrasting phrases between general existence and specific existence.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the passage states that general existence does not need a cause, and that only specific existence (with possibility) needs a cause. Underline these statements.
The key terms are: وجود عام (general existence) and وجود مخصص (specific existence).
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: from the necessity of a cause for the specific, to the role of the Creator's knowledge and will, and finally to the specification of existence. Pay attention to how the passage connects knowledge, will, and existence.
The passage ends with the specification of thingness and substantiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا فإن الوجود ليس يفتقر إلى الموجد، وإلا فيلزم وجود القديم بل وجود مخصص هو بصفة الإمكان يفتقر إلى الموجد، ثم الموجد الممكن من حيث هو عام لا يتحقق له وجود، بل وجود ممكن هو بصفة كذا أو كذا، ويتحقق له وجود فيثبت أن المعين المشار إليه هو المفتقر إلى الموجد، لكن لا يتحقق له وجود إلا ويريده الموجد، وليس يريده الموجد إلا ويعلمه قبل إيجاده فيتخصص وجوده وجودًا عرضًا جوهرًا في علم الموجد، فالمعلوم يتخصص مرادا، والمراد يتخصص وجودًا هو بعينه جوهر إلا أنه في ذاته يكون شيئًا فيخصصه الوجود بعد الشيء، حتى تتخصص الشيئية الخارجة جوهرًا، وتتخصص الجوهرية العامة الخارجة بهذا الجوهر.
And also: Existence does not need the cause, otherwise the existence of the eternal would follow; rather, a specific existence that has the attribute of possibility needs the cause. Then the possible cause insofar as it is general has no existence realized for it; rather, a possible existence that has the attribute of such-and-such, and its existence is realized, so it is established that the determinate referred-to is what needs the cause, but its existence is not realized except when the cause wills it, and the cause does not will it except when He knows it before its bringing into existence, so its existence becomes specified as an existence, an accident, a substance in the knowledge of the cause. Thus, the known becomes specified as willed, and the willed becomes specified as existence that is itself a substance, except that in its essence it is a thing, so existence specifies it after the thing, until the thingness external becomes substance, and the general substantiality external becomes specified by this substance.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage argues that existence in general (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq) does not need a cause, otherwise eternal existence would also need a cause, which is impossible. Rather, it is specific existence that is contingent (bi-ṣifat al-imkān) and needs a cause. The possible cause (al-mūjid al-mumkin) insofar as it is general has no realized existence; only a specific possible existence (with such-and-such attributes) is realized. Hence, the determinate particular (al-mu‘ayyan al-mushār ilayhi) is what needs the cause. Its existence is only realized when the cause wills it, and the cause only wills it after knowing it before its creation. In the knowledge of the creator, its existence becomes specified as an existence, an accident, or a substance. Thus, the known specifies as willed, and the willed specifies as existence which is identical to the substance. The existence then specifies the thing after the thingness, so that the external thingness becomes a substance, and the general substantiality external becomes specified by this particular substance. This shows that the specific existence depends entirely on the creator's knowledge and will.

Key Terms
وجود عام
وجود مخصص
إمكان
موجد
معين
شيئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That existence in general needs a cause, like specific things do.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only particularized existence is contingent; general existence does not need a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does general existence not need a cause?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because if it did, the eternal would also need a cause, which is impossible.
C) Because it is not possible.
D) Because it is not a thing.

Key Takeaway
General existence is not contingent, but every specific existence is dependent on the Creator's knowledge and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson insists that every specific moment of your existence is known and willed by the Creator before it happens. Have you ever truly felt dependent on Him for even your next breath? What would change if you lived that reality? Commit to one specific act today that acknowledges His will over you.
?
The passage teaches that general existence needs no cause, but your particular existence does. Are there areas of your life where you act as if your specific existence is self-sufficient—your health, wealth, relationships? Identify one such area and mentally surrender it to the One who alone gives it reality.

Existence as the Most General Attribute and Causal Implications
Argument that existence is the most general attribute and that the cause gives specific natures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence is the most general attribute of existents and why the cause must give both existence and specific natures.

Core Idea
Existence is the general canvas; the cause paints the specific.

This passage builds on the foundational principle that existence is the most general attribute. It then explores a critical implication: if the cause only gives existence, things would exist without being specifically what they are. The argument uses whiteness and blackness as examples to show that the cause must be responsible for specific natures, and that the specific entails the general. This reasoning is central to the Kalam understanding of divine causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the general principle to the specific example and its logical conclusions.
The passage is dense; focus on the cause's role.
2
Identify the key claims
List the main claims: (1) existence is the most general attribute, (2) the general does not necessitate the specific, (3) the cause must give the specific nature, (4) the specific necessitates the general, (5) opposition of specific natures negates existence.
3
Analyze the example of whiteness
Consider the hypothetical: if whiteness were attributed only to existence from the cause, it would be existent but not white. This shows the cause must give the 'whiteness' itself.
The reversal case confirms the cause gives the specific.
4
Understand the logical necessity
Note that the existence of the specific (whiteness) necessarily implies the existence of the general (existence). Also, the specific natures (whiteness and blackness) are opposed, so negating one specific negates existence entirely.
This follows the principle that the specific cannot exist without the general.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating the non-existent is devoid of existence, just as the cause exists. Connect this to the overall argument about causation.
This reinforces that existence and specific natures come from the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا فإن الوجود أعم صفات الموجودات، وإيجاد الأعم لا يوجب وجود الأخص، فلو كان البياض مثلا منتسباً إلى الموجد من حيث وجوده فقط لكان يكون موجودًا لا بياضاً، بل لو عكس الأمر وقلب الحال فقبل أوجده سوادًا أو بياضا. وانتسبت البياضية إلى الموجد فقط، ولكن من ضرورة وجود الأخص وجود الأعم كان أمثل من حيث العقل، ولذلك نقول: إن البياض يضاد السواد ببياضيته، فإذا انتفى السوادية انتفى الوجود؛ إذ ليس من قضية العقل انتفاء السوادية وبقاء الوجود، وعند القوم أن المعدوم عادم لوجوده، كما أن الموجد.
And also: Existence is the most general attribute of existents, and the bringing into existence of the general does not necessitate the existence of the specific. So if whiteness, for example, were attributed to the cause only in terms of its existence, it would be existent not white. Rather, if the matter were reversed and the situation inverted, we would accept that He created it black or white. And whiteness is attributed to the cause only, but from the necessity of the existence of the specific is the existence of the general; that is more appropriate from the perspective of reason. Therefore we say: Whiteness is opposed to blackness by its whiteness, so if blackness is negated, existence is negated; for it is not a requirement of reason to negate blackness and preserve existence. And according to the people, the non-existent is devoid of its existence, just as the cause [is found].
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage argues that existence is the most general attribute of existents (a‘amm ṣifāt al-mawjūdāt). Creating the general (existence) does not necessitate the existence of the specific (like whiteness). If whiteness were attributed to the cause only in terms of its existence, then it would exist but not be white. Rather, the cause must be responsible for the specific nature (bayāḍiyya). Conversely, reason dictates that the existence of the specific necessitates the existence of the general (i.e., if whiteness exists, then existence exists). Moreover, whiteness opposes blackness by its own nature; if blackness is negated, existence is also negated because the specific cannot exist without the general. The passage ends with a reference to the view of 'the people' (al-qawm) that the non-existent lacks its existence, just as the cause [exists] – possibly meaning the cause exists while the effect does not.

Key Terms
أعم صفات (most general attribute)
أخص (specific)
بياض (whiteness)
سواد (blackness)
معدوم (non-existent)
موجد (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'the general does not necessitate the specific' is sometimes misunderstood to mean that specific things could exist without the general (existence).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the general (existence) does not necessitate the specific in terms of causation—the cause must give the specific nature—but logically, the specific always entails the general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if whiteness were only given existence by the cause, what would it be?
A) It would be white but not exist.
B) It would exist but not be white.
C) It would be both existent and white.
D) It would be neither existent nor white.

Key Takeaway
The cause must give both existence and specific natures, and the specific necessarily implies the general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If existence is the most general attribute and the cause gives specific natures, then what specific 'whiteness' in your life have you attributed to chance rather than to the Creator? Will you acknowledge Him as the direct cause of both your existence and every specific quality you possess?
?
The passage argues that negating the specific negates existence. In your spiritual life, are there specific qualities (like patience or gratitude) that you have neglected, thereby risking a negation of your very spiritual existence? What specific quality will you recommit to restoring today?
?
You have been given both existence and a specific nature by the Cause. If your specific nature (your personality, talents, challenges) is directly from Him, how does that change your attitude toward those aspects of yourself you dislike? Can you accept them as His deliberate creation and use them in His service?

The Hylists' First Group: First Principles and the Origin of Bodies
Describes the first group of Hylists' view on first principles and the origin of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first group of Hylists' view on the first principles and the origin of bodies, including the role of hyle as a bare substrate that becomes a composite body through the form of three dimensions.

Core Idea
The Hylists see matter as a bare potential that becomes body through the form of dimensions.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari introduces the first group of Hylists, who posit a hyle (prime matter) as one of the first principles. They consider it a bare substrate that, upon receiving the form of three dimensions, becomes a composite body. This view blends Neoplatonic and Aristotelian ideas into Islamic theological cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's description of the Hylists' view come through.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the first group described.
2
Identify the first principles
Notice that the Hylists list intellect, soul, and hyle as first principles, and some include the Creator. Determine whether the hyle is the third or fourth principle.
The passage states it is either third or fourth.
3
Understand the process from hyle to body
Trace how the hyle, initially stripped of all forms, receives the first form (three dimensions) and becomes a composite body possessing length, width, and depth.
Before form, the hyle only has preparedness for reception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أصحاب الهيولي فافترقت فيها على طريقين فرقتين : أحدهما إثبات هيولي للعالم مجردة عن الصور وعدوها من المبادي الأول وهي ثالثها أو رابعها ، فقالوا : المبادي هي العقل والنفس والهيولي، وقالوا : المبادي هو الباري سبحانه والعقل والنفس والهيولي وهي كانت مجردة عن الصور كلها فحدثت الصورة الأولى وهي الأبعاد الثلاثة فصارت جسما مركبًا ذا طول وعرض وعمق، ولم يكن لها قبل الصورة إلا استعداد لمجرد القبول، فإذا حدثت الصورة
As for the people of Hyle (ashab al-hayula), they divided into two groups, two sects: one of them is the affirmation of a hyle for the world stripped of forms, and they counted it among the first principles (mabadi'), and it is the third or fourth of them. They said: the first principles are intellect ('aql), soul (nafs), and hyle (hayula). And they said: the first principles are the Creator (al-bari), exalted is He, and intellect, soul, and hyle. And it (the hyle) was stripped of all forms, then the first form (sura) arose, which is the three dimensions (al-ab'ad al-thalatha), so it became a composite body (jism murakkab) possessing length, width, and depth. And before the form, it had only a preparedness (isti'dad) for mere reception (qabul). Then when the form arose...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage introduces the first group of Hylists, who posit a hyle (prime matter) for the world stripped of all forms. They consider this hyle as one of the first principles, either the third or fourth, depending on whether the Creator is included. Their list of principles includes intellect, soul, and hyle, and some add the Creator as the first. The hyle initially is bare of all forms; then the first form arises, which is the three dimensions (length, width, depth), and this makes a composite body. Before receiving form, the hyle had only a bare capacity for reception. The passage breaks off mid-sentence as it begins to describe what happens when the form arises, likely continuing to explain the emergence of the physical world. This view is influenced by Neoplatonic or Aristotelian cosmology, adapted into Islamic philosophical theology.

Key Terms
الهيولي
المبادي
العقل
النفس
الصورة
الأبعاد الثلاثة
الجسم المركب
الاستعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hyle is a material substrate that pre-exists as a distinct thing alongside forms.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the hyle is a principle stripped of forms, having only a preparedness for reception.

Checkpoint
According to the first group of Hylists, what is the first form that arises from the hyle?
A) The soul
B) The three dimensions (length, width, depth)
C) The intellect
D) The Creator

Key Takeaway
The first group of Hylists views the hyle as a bare potentiality that becomes a composite body upon receiving the form of three dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a preparedness (isti'dad) to receive forms of knowledge and faith. What 'form' from Allah are you currently resisting because you cling to your own notion of potential? Will you allow the first form—submission to the Creator—to shape your composite self into a true servant?
?
The Hylists reduced the world to intellect, soul, and matter. Do you reduce your purpose to the material or intellectual without the transcendent? If your life were stripped of all forms like the hyle, what form of worship would you be ready to receive? Make a concrete intention to receive a new form of obedience today.

Two Ways of Conceiving Hyle: Actual vs. Mental
Two ways of conceiving the stages of hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways of conceiving the stages of material composition: as a real sequential process and as a purely mental abstraction.

Core Idea
Hyle's stages are either actual or only in the mind.

This passage from al-Shahrastani's Nihayat al-Aqdam presents two competing conceptions of material substance. The first sees a real hierarchy from prime matter to elements to composites. The second insists that this ordering is merely a conceptual analysis, not found in external existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the relationship between matter and form in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the two ways.
2
Identify the first way
Notice the sequential stages: second hyle, four qualities, elements, composites. This is presented as an actual process.
3
Identify the second way
Understand the argument that this order is only mental, not actual. The second group affirms hyle not stripped of form.
4
Compare the two views
Ask what is at stake: is hyle ever found without form in reality? The first way says yes, the second says no.
5
Reflect on the implications
This sets up a key debate about the nature of matter and whether matter can exist independently of form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صارت بالفعل موجودة وهي الهيولي الثانية، ثم إذا لحقها الكيفيات الأربعة التي هي الحرارة والبرود الفاعلتان والرطوبة واليبوسة المنفعلتان، صارت الأركان التي هي النار والماء والهواء والأرض وهي الهيولي الثالثة ثم تتكون منها المركبات هي التي تلحقها الأعراض وهي الكون والفساد ويكون بعضها هيولي بعض. الطريق الثاني: هو أنهم أثبتوا الهيولي غير مجردة عن الصورة قالوا: وهذا الترتيب الذي ذكرناه مقدر في العقل والوهم خاصة دون الوجود، وليس في الوجود جوهر مطلق قابل للأبعاد ثم يلحقه الأبعاد، ولا جسم عار عن الكيفيات، ثم عرضت له الكيفيات، وإنما هو عندما نظرنا في ما هو أقدم بالطبع وأبسط في الوهم والعقل.
It actually became existing, and it is the second hyle. Then when it acquires the four qualities which are heat and cold (the active ones) and moisture and dryness (the passive ones), it becomes the elements which are fire, water, air, and earth—and that is the third hyle. Then from them are formed the composites which are subject to accidents, which are generation and corruption, and some of them become hyle for others. The second way: They (the other group) affirmed hyle not stripped of form, saying: This arrangement we mentioned is estimated in the intellect and estimation alone, not in existence. There does not exist in existence an absolute substance receptive of dimensions then dimensions attach to it, nor a body devoid of qualities then qualities attach to it. Rather, it is only when we consider what is prior by nature and simpler in estimation and intellect.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage describes the two ways of conceiving the nature of hyle (matter) in the context of the composition of physical bodies. The first way, presented by the author, details a hierarchy: starting from the second hyle (prime matter with some actuality), then acquiring the four primary qualities (hot, cold, moist, dry) to become the four elements (fire, water, air, earth) which is the third hyle, and finally from these elements composites arise, subject to generation and corruption. This is a sequential actual process. The second way, attributed to those who affirm hyle not stripped of form, argues that this order is only a mental consideration, not actual. In reality, there is no substance that first exists without dimensions and then acquires them, nor a body without qualities that later receives them. Instead, our intellect abstractly considers what is prior by nature and simpler in estimation.

Key Terms
الهيولي (hyle)
الكيفيات الأربعة (the four qualities)
الأركان (elements)
المركبات (composites)
الوجود (existence)
العقل (intellect)
الوهم (estimation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the second way denies the reality of hyle altogether.
Correction: The second way does not deny hyle; it denies that hyle exists as a separate, formless stage in external reality. Hyle always exists with form; the stages are only mental abstractions.

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of the second way claim about the order of composition?
A) It is actual and sequential in existence.
B) It is only estimated in intellect and imagination, not in existence.
C) It begins with prime matter stripped of all form.
D) The elements are not composed of matter.

Key Takeaway
The stages of material composition are either actual sequential steps or merely conceptual frameworks in the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you analyze your own beliefs about how the world is composed, do you treat conceptual stages (like 'matter first, then form') as if they were separate realities? What would change if you adopted the second way—seeing them as intellectual tools rather than actual steps?
?
In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes imagine a sequence of changes (e.g., from sinfulness to righteousness) as if they must happen in a fixed order? How does knowing that such order may be only mental estimation free you to trust God's direct action?
?
If the stages of matter are only estimated in the intellect, what does that say about your own reasoning? Is your intellect capable of grasping reality as it is, or does it impose its own order? How does this humility affect the way you approach theological truths?

The Substance Beneath Change
First part of the proof: substance distinct from form from change of connection/separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the reception of alternating attributes requires a substance distinct from the corporeal form.

Core Idea
What remains when opposites alternate is not the opposite itself.

Kalām begins with what is directly observable: bodies undergo change. Before delving into divine attributes, we must grasp the metaphysical groundwork—what underlies physical change. The proponents of abstract hyle present a simple but powerful proof: the recipient of connection and separation cannot be connection or separation themselves, for the recipient persists while one of them disappears. This lesson unpacks that foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source once, then the translation. Notice how the argument moves from observation (bodies are receptive) to inference (there must be a substance that is not the form).
The key term is 'qābil' (recipient).
2
Identify the premise
The passage states: every body is subject to connection and separation. Ask yourself: what does it mean for a body to 'receive' connection? Is connection itself the same as the body?
This is the crucial distinction between substrate and accident.
3
Trace the inference
Because the recipient (the substance) remains when connection changes to separation, it cannot be identical to either. Therefore, there exists a substance other than the corporeal form.
This is a direct application of the principle that opposites cannot inhere in the same subject at the same time.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This reasoning establishes that bodies are not self-sufficient; they depend on a substrate. This connects to later arguments about the contingency of the world and the necessity of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب الهيولي المجردة: أما إثبات الهيولي لكل فأمر معقول محقق بالبرهان، وذلك أن كل جسم قابل للاتصال والانفصال والتصور والتشكل بالصورة والأشكال، ومعلوم أن قابل الاتصال والانفصال أمر وراء الاتصال والانفصال، فإن القابل يبقى بطريان أحدهما والاتصال لا يبقى بعد طريان الانفصال وبالعكس فظاهر أن هاهنا جوهر غير الصورة الجسمية يعرض له الاتصال والانفصال معا. ثم قالوا: ما من انفصال واتصال معين إلا ويمكن زواله، وما من شكل وصورة وحيز وجهة إلا وهو عارض لذلك الجوهر، فيمكن أن يتعرى عن جميع الصور كما أمكن أن يتعرى عن كل صورة، بل يجب أن يكون مبدأ الأجسام المركبة جوهر غير مركب، فإن كل مركب لو كان عن مركب لتسلسل إلى غير النهاية، فتركب القميص من الغزل والغزل من القطن، والقطن من الأركان، والأركان من العناصر وهي الهيولي القابلة للصور والكيفيات، فإذا انحلت التركيبات فهي البسائط، أو إذا انحلت البسائط فهي الهيولي المجردة عن كل صورة القابلة لكل صورة.
The proponents of abstract hyle said: As for affirming hyle for everything, it is an intelligible matter verified by proof. That is because every body is susceptible to connection and separation, to being imagined and shaped by form and shapes. It is known that the recipient of connection and separation is something beyond connection and separation, for the recipient remains when one of them occurs, while connection does not remain after the occurrence of separation and vice versa. So it is apparent that there is here a substance other than the corporeal form, to which connection and separation both occur. Then they said: There is no particular separation or connection except that it is possible for it to cease, and no shape, form, place, or direction except that it is an accident to that substance. So it is possible for it to be stripped of all forms, just as it is possible for it to be stripped of every single form. Rather, it must be that the principle of composite bodies is a substance that is not composite, for if every composite were from a composite, there would be an infinite regress. Thus, the shirt is composed of thread, thread from cotton, cotton from the elements, and the elements from the primary matters which are the hyle receptive of forms and qualities. So when the composites are resolved, they become simples, or when the simples are resolved, they become the abstract hyle stripped of all form, receptive of every form.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from the advocates of abstract hyle (prime matter devoid of form). The proof begins with the observation that every body can undergo connection (ittisāl) and separation (infiṣāl), and can receive different shapes and forms. The recipient of these changes must be distinct from the changes themselves, because the recipient persists while the changes alternate. Therefore, there exists a substance (jawhar) other than the corporeal form, which is the substratum for both connection and separation. Next, they argue that any specific connection or separation can be removed, as can any shape, form, place, or direction. Since these are all accidents (aʿrāḍ) to that substance, it is possible for the substance to be stripped of all forms entirely. Furthermore, the ultimate principle of composite bodies must be a simple substance (jawhar ghayr murakkab) to avoid infinite regress. They provide an example: a shirt is from thread, thread from cotton, cotton from elements, and elements from primary matters (hyle). Decomposition leads back to simples, and further to abstract hyle, which is receptive of all forms.

Key Terms
الهيولي المجردة
الجوهر
الاتصال والانفصال
الصورة الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the corporeal form itself can change without a substrate.
Correction: The argument shows that form cannot change while remaining identical; a change from one form to another requires a persisting substrate that is not itself a form.

Checkpoint
Why must there be a substance distinct from the corporeal form?
A) Because connection and separation cannot exist simultaneously.
B) Because the recipient remains while connection and separation alternate.
C) Because the corporeal form is simple.
D) Because bodies are subject to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The alternation of opposite attributes in a body proves the existence of a substance that is not the body's form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe your identity is stable, but what in you constantly changes—your body, thoughts, emotions? If there is a substance that remains beneath all that change, what does that say about your attachment to changing forms? Can you point to anything in your daily life that you treat as if it will last, even though it clearly alternates like connection and separation?
?
The proof shows that a recipient must exist for opposites to alternate. What 'opposites' are alternating in your heart right now—hope and despair, faith and doubt? Are you aware of a deeper 'recipient' that remains through those states? How would your life change if you anchored yourself in that recipient rather than the passing moods?
?
If the body's forms are temporary, and only the underlying substance endures, what does that imply about your pursuit of physical beauty, health, or status? Are you investing in what passes away or in what remains? This lesson forces you to choose: will you continue to cling to changing forms, or will you seek the substance that outlasts them?

Stripping All Forms and the Infinite Regress
Second part: stripping all forms and infinite regress to a simple substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the substrate for change must be able to be stripped of all forms and why composite bodies ultimately require a simple substance.

Core Idea
To avoid infinite regress, the ultimate substrate must be simple.

Having established that bodies have a substrate distinct from form, the proponents of abstract hyle now push further. If any particular form can be removed, why cannot all forms be removed? And if every composite is made of smaller composites, we would regress infinitely—so there must be a simple substance, which they identify as abstract hyle, receptive of all forms. This lesson examines these deeper implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ثم قالوا' to the end, along with the translation. Note the two arguments: (1) the possibility of stripping all forms, (2) the infinite regress argument.
The Arabic uses 'yajibu an yakūna' to indicate necessity.
2
Analyze the stripping argument
Since every specific connection, separation, shape, place, and direction can be removed from the substance, it follows that the substance can be stripped of all such forms simultaneously. This is a logical inference from particular to universal possibility.
The assumption is that the substrate is not essentially tied to any particular accident.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
If every composite were composed of further composites, we would never reach a foundation. The example of shirt → thread → cotton → elements → hyle shows a chain that must terminate in a simple substance that is not itself composite.
This is a classic philosophical argument for an ultimate substratum.
4
Connect to the broader kalām framework
The simple substance, abstract hyle, is posited as the material cause of all bodies, which connects to discussions about creation and the contingent nature of the physical world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The proponents of abstract hyle said: As for affirming hyle for everything, it is an intelligible matter verified by proof. That is because every body is susceptible to connection and separation, to being imagined and shaped by form and shapes. It is known that the recipient of connection and separation is something beyond connection and separation, for the recipient remains when one of them occurs, while connection does not remain after the occurrence of separation and vice versa. So it is apparent that there is here a substance other than the corporeal form, to which connection and separation both occur. Then they said: There is no particular separation or connection except that it is possible for it to cease, and no shape, form, place, or direction except that it is an accident to that substance. So it is possible for it to be stripped of all forms, just as it is possible for it to be stripped of every single form. Rather, it must be that the principle of composite bodies is a substance that is not composite, for if every composite were from a composite, there would be an infinite regress. Thus, the shirt is composed of thread, thread from cotton, cotton from the elements, and the elements from the primary matters which are the hyle receptive of forms and qualities. So when the composites are resolved, they become simples, or when the simples are resolved, they become the abstract hyle stripped of all form, receptive of every form.
Source pages p. 163

This passage presents the argument from the advocates of abstract hyle (prime matter devoid of form). The proof begins with the observation that every body can undergo connection (ittisāl) and separation (infiṣāl), and can receive different shapes and forms. The recipient of these changes must be distinct from the changes themselves, because the recipient persists while the changes alternate. Therefore, there exists a substance (jawhar) other than the corporeal form, which is the substratum for both connection and separation. Objection to Abstract Hyle and Two Responses
Objection to abstract hyle with philosopher's and theologian's answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection to abstract hyle and two distinct responses: one from a philosopher distinguishing substance from accident, and another from a theologian questioning the logical inference.

Core Idea
What changes in an accident does not extend to the substance itself.

This lesson examines a key objection to the concept of abstract hyle—the idea that matter can exist without any form. Proponents of 'hyle with form' argue that the possibility of stripping particular accidents does not imply the possibility of stripping all forms, because substances and accidents are fundamentally different. Two answers are presented: one from a philosopher distinguishing substance from accident, and another from a theologian challenging the logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Absorb the objection and the two responses.
Notice the distinction between 'hyle with form' and 'abstract hyle'.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: proponents of hyle with form concede that hyle is an intelligible substance, but deny that it can be stripped of all forms. They argue the premises are arbitrary.
The objection targets the inference from particular to general stripping.
3
Analyze the philosopher's answer
The philosopher distinguishes between accidents (which change) and the corporeal form (a substance which does not change). Thus, removing a particular accident does not imply removing the substance.
Key distinction: 'what is an accident may undergo change, but what is a substance has never changed.'
4
Examine the theologian's answer
The theologian simply questions the inference: why does freedom from one accident imply freedom from all? This is a direct challenge to the logical step.
This is a skeptical response, not a positive proof.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the concept of hyle and for theological cosmology. How do these answers protect the idea that matter always requires form?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب الهيولي مع الصورة: أما إثبات الهيولي جوهرًا معقولا فمسلم للعقل، وأما جواز تعريها عن الصور أو وجوب ذلك فهو المختلف فيه، وما المقدمات تحكمات. فلم قلتم: إنه إذا أمكن تعريها عن اتصال وانفصال معين أمكن تعريها عن كل اتصال وانفصال؟ لأن الذي يتبدل ويتغير من الاتصال والانفصال عرض من باب الكم، والاتصال الذي هو الأبعاد الثلاثة جوهر هو صورة جسمية، فما هو عرض يجوز عليه التبدل وما هو جوهر لم يتبدل قط، وليس العرض من قبيل الجوهر حتى يحكم عليه بحكم الثاني. وكذلك كون الجسم في حيز مخصوص من قبيل الأعراض من باب الأين، وكونه متحيزا صورة جسمية هو من قبيل الجواهر، فلم يجز أن يقال: إذا جاز عليه تبدل المكان المخصوص جاز عليه أن لا يكون له حيز ومكان. هذا جواب الحكيم للحكيم. وأما جواب المتكلم فيقول: لمَ إذا جاز خلو الجوهر عن عرض جاز خلوه عن كل عرض؟
The proponents of hyle with form said: As for affirming hyle as an intelligible substance, it is conceded by reason. But as for the permissibility of stripping it of forms or the necessity of that, that is what is disputed. The premises are arbitrary. Why do you say that if it is possible to strip it of a particular connection and separation, it is possible to strip it of all connection and separation? Because that which changes and alters among connection and separation is an accident from the category of quantity, while connection which is the three dimensions is a substance, namely the corporeal form. So what is an accident may undergo change, but what is a substance has never changed. An accident is not of the same genus as a substance such that it could be judged by the same judgment. Likewise, the body's being in a particular place is from the category of accidents (al-ayn), while its being extended in space is a corporeal form from the category of substances. So it is not permissible to say: if it is possible for it to change a particular place, it is possible for it to have no place at all. This is the answer of the philosopher to the philosopher. As for the answer of the theologian, he says: Why, if it is possible for a substance to be free from one accident, is it possible for it to be free from every accident?
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents the objection from those who hold that hyle always exists with form (hayula ma'a al-sura). They concede that the concept of hyle as an intelligible substance is reasonable, but they reject the claim that it can be stripped of all forms. They argue that the proponents of abstract hyle have made arbitrary premises. Specifically, they challenge the inference that because a particular connection or separation can be removed, therefore all connections and separations can be removed. Their reasoning distinguishes between accidents and substances: connection and separation as accidents (from the category of quantity) can change, but the three-dimensional extension (al-abʿād al-thalātha) is a substance (the corporeal form) which does not change. One cannot transfer the possibility of change from an accident to a substance. Similarly, being in a specific place is an accident (al-ayn), but being extended in space is a substance. So removing a particular place does not imply possibility of having no place. This is the answer of the philosopher (al-ḥakīm) to the philosopher. Then the answer of the theologian (al-mutakallim) is given: He simply questions the inference: why does the possibility of being free from one accident imply possibility of being free from all accidents? This is a theological response that rejects the logical step.

Key Terms
الهيولي مع الصورة (hyle with form)
العرض (accident)
الجوهر (substance)
الكم (quantity)
الأين (place category)
الحكيم (philosopher)
المتكلم (theologian)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a substance can lose some accidents, it can lose all accidents.
Correction: The passage corrects this by showing that what changes is an accident, while the substantial corporeal form remains unchanged. The possibility of change in accidents does not apply to the substance itself.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers defending hyle with form, why can't we infer that a substance can be stripped of all forms from its being stripped of a particular form?
A) Because the substance is always changing.
B) Because what changes is an accident, while the substance (corporeal form) remains unchanged.
C) Because all forms are necessary.
D) Because infinite regress would occur.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between substance and accident is crucial: the possibility of change in accidents does not transfer to the substance, so abstract hyle is not proven by the removal of particular forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual journey, do you ever confuse the changing accidents of your life (e.g., fluctuating emotions, failures in worship) with your unchanging substance (your fitrah, your covenant with Allah)? How does this lesson's distinction between what changes and what does not change call you to re-evaluate your self-perception?
?
The theologian questions the leap from 'free from one accident' to 'free from all'. Think of a time when you justified neglecting multiple obligations because you failed in one. How does this passage challenge you to examine the logic of your own excuses before Allah?
?
If you were to strip away all the 'accidents' of your daily routine – your busyness, your habits, your comfort – what would remain of your substance? Are you cultivating that enduring core, or are you only managing the changing surface?

From Shirt to Prime Matter: The Hierarchy of Composition
Traces composition from shirt to prime matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchy of material composition from everyday objects down to prime matter, and the distinction between composite bodies and simple substances.

Core Idea
Everything physical is composed from prime matter and form.

This passage from al-Shahrastani presents a foundational model of the physical world in Islamic kalam. It traces the composition of an ordinary shirt back through thread, cotton, atoms, elements, and ultimately to prime matter. This model underlies discussions about change, substance, and the limits of analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the argument.
Notice the chain: shirt → thread → cotton → atoms → elements → prime matter.
2
Trace the hierarchy step by step
Go through each level of composition. Ask: what is each thing made of? Follow the chain until you reach the ultimate substrate.
3
Understand 'simple substances' and 'prime matter'
The passage distinguishes between composite bodies, simple substances (basa'it), and prime matter. Reflect on what each term means in this context.
Prime matter is not observable; it is a philosophical concept.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why al-Shahrastani starts with a concrete example. How does this hierarchy support arguments about creation and change?
This passage sets up the idea that all change involves form and matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى غير النهاية، فتركب القميص من الغزل والغزل من القطن، والقطن من الأركان، والأركان من العناصر وهي الهيولي القابلة للصور والكيفيات، فإذا انحلت التركيبات فهي البسائط ، أو إذا انحلت البسائط فهي الهيولي المجردة عن كل صورة القابلة لكل صورة.
To infinity, so the shirt is composed of thread, and thread from cotton, and cotton from atoms, and atoms from elements, which are prime matter (hayula) receptive of forms and qualities. When compositions are dissolved, they become simple substances, or when simple substances are dissolved, they become prime matter stripped of every form, receptive of every form.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a description of the hierarchy of composition in the physical world. It illustrates how composite objects like a shirt are made of thread, thread from cotton, cotton from atoms (arkan), atoms from elements, and elements from prime matter (hayula) which is the underlying substrate receptive of forms and qualities. The author explains that when composites are broken down, we get simple substances (basa'it), and when simple substances are further broken down, we get prime matter stripped of all form but receptive of all forms. This sets the stage for the debate about whether prime matter can exist without any form.

Prime Matter: Can It Be Stripped of All Forms?
Philosophers argue prime matter can be stripped of all forms; answer distinguishes essential and accidental aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers defended the view that prime matter retains essential attributes (like three-dimensional extension) even when stripped of accidental forms.

Core Idea
Changing accidents does not change the substance; essential attributes remain.

This passage presents a philosophical exchange about prime matter. A group of philosophers argued that if prime matter can lose a specific form, it can lose all forms. The answer refutes this by distinguishing between accidents, which can change, and essential substantial attributes, which are permanent. Let's explore this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the claim, then the answer.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core claim
The philosophers claim: if it is possible to strip prime matter of a specific connection/separation, then it is possible to strip it of all connection and separation. Why do they think that?
3
Understand the refutation
The answer distinguishes between accidents (like specific quantity or location) and substances (like the three dimensions as a corporeal form). Accidents change, substances do not.
The key is that not all attributes are the same kind.
4
Apply the distinction to a second example
Similarly, being in a particular place is an accident, but being extended is a substance. So changing place does not imply losing extension entirely.
This shows the fallacy of the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب الهيولي مع الصورة: أما إثبات الهيولي جوهرًا معقولا فمسلم للعقل، وأما جواز تعريها عن الصور أو وجوب ذلك فهو المختلف فيه، وما المقدمات تحكمات، فلِمَ قلتم : إنه إذا أمكن تعريها عن اتصال وانفصال معين أمكن تعريها عن كل اتصال وانفصال ، لأن الذي يتبدل ويتغير " من الاتصال والانفصال عرض من باب الكم، والاتصال الذي هو الأبعاد الثلاثة جوهر هو صورة جسمية، فما هو عرض يجوز عليه التبدل وما هو جوهر لم يتبدل قط، وليس العرض من قبيل الجوهر حتى يحكم عليه بحكم الثاني، وكذلك كون الجسم في حيز مخصوص من قبيل الأعراض من باب الأين، وكونه متحيزا صورة جسمية هو من قبيل الجواهر، فلم يجز أن يقال: إذا جاز عليه تبدل المكان المخصوص جاز عليه أن لا يكون له حيز ومكان، هذا جواب الحكيم للحكيم.
The proponents of prime matter with form said: As for affirming prime matter as an intellectual substance, that is accepted by reason, but as for the permissibility of stripping it of forms or the necessity of that, it is disputed. The premises are arbitrary. So why do you say: if it is possible to strip it of a specific connection and separation, it is possible to strip it of all connection and separation; because that which changes and alters from connection and separation is an accident from the category of quantity, while the connection which is the three dimensions is a substance and is a corporeal form. So what is an accident is subject to change, and what is a substance never changes, and an accident is not of the genus of substance so that it could be judged by the ruling of the latter. Similarly, the body's being in a particular place is from the category of accidents (the category of 'where'), while its being extended is a corporeal form and is from the category of substances. So it cannot be said: if it is subject to change of a particular place, then it is subject to not having a place at all. This is the answer of the philosopher to the philosopher.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from a group of philosophers (as'hab al-hayula ma'a al-sura) who affirm prime matter as an intellectual substance. They raise an objection: they claim that if it is possible to strip prime matter of a particular accident (like a specific connection or separation), then it should be possible to strip it of all connections and separations. The answer (jawab al-hakim) refutes this by distinguishing between accidents (like specific quantity) and essential substances (like the three dimensions which are a corporeal form). The accident can change, but the substantial form does not change. Similarly, being in a particular place is an accident, but having extension (tahayyuz) is a substance. So the analogy fails. This is the philosopher's answer to his fellow philosopher.

الأبعاد الثلاثة
الصورة الجسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If prime matter can lose a specific accidental form (like a particular shape), then it can lose all forms, including essential ones.
Correction: The answer shows that accidental forms (like particular quantity or place) can change, but essential substantial forms (like three-dimensional extension as a corporeal form) are permanent and not subject to change.

Checkpoint

A) Because accidents are independent
B) Because some attributes are essential and do not change
C) Because prime matter cannot have accidents
D) Because all accidents are the same

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that accidental attributes (e.g., specific quantity or location) can change, but essential attributes (e.g., three-dimensional extension as a substance) are permanent; thus, stripping prime matter of one accident does not imply stripping it of all properties.

Why Substance Cannot Be Stripped of All Accidents
Theologian argues substance can be stripped of some accidents but not all; distinguishes subsistence from existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologian's distinction between a substance's subsistence (qiyam bi al-dhat) and its existence (wujud), and why it must have at least one accident—namely, being in a place.

Core Idea
Substance is self-subsistent but never found without the accident of place.

The theologian (mutakallim) responds to a philosopher's objection: if a substance can lose one accident, why not all? He argues that while the substance does not need accidents for its own subsistence, in actual existence it cannot be imagined without occupying a specific location. This preserves the definition of substance as self-subsistent while acknowledging its embodied reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the first question posed by the theologian.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection: if substance can be free from one accident, why not all? The theologian responds with a distinction between subsistence and existence.
The theologian does not deny that substance can lose some accidents.
3
Understand the distinction
Substance is self-subsistent (qaim bi dhatihi) and does not need accidents to be a substance. However, in existence it must have at least the accident of place (kawn fi makan).
This resolves the confusion between substance and accident definitions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The theologian concludes that substance cannot be free of all accidents because existence requires place, but subsistence does not depend on accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما جواب المتكلم فيقول : لمَ إذا جاز خلو الجوهر عن عرض جاز خلوه عن كل عرض؟ قال : لأن الجوهر هو القائم بذاته المستغني عن المحل، فلو لم يجز خلوه عن الأعراض لبطل قيامه بذاته، ولكن في وجوده محتاجًا إلى العرض، كما كان العرض في وجوده محتاجاً إلى الجوهر، وحينئذ يلتبس حد الجوهر بحد العرض، ويختلط أحدهما بالآخر، وذلك خروج عن المعقول. فيقول المتكلم : الجوهر في قيامه بذاته يستغني عن محل يحل فيه ؛ بحيث يوصف المحل،به والعرض في احتياجه من حيث ذاته يحتاج إلى محل يحل فيه ؛ بحيث يوصف به، لكن لا يجوز أن يخلو الجوهر عن كل الأعراض، لا لأنه يحتاج إليها في قيامه بذاته ،جوهرًا، لكن في وجوده لا يتصور أن يكون خاليا عن كون في مكان مخصوص.
As for the theologian's answer, he says: Why, if it is permissible for the substance to be free from an accident, is it permissible for it to be free from every accident? He said: Because the substance is self-subsistent, independent of a substrate. If it were not permissible for it to be free of accidents, its self-subsistence would be invalidated. But in its existence it is in need of the accident, just as the accident in its existence is in need of the substance. Then the definition of substance would be confused with the definition of accident, and one would be mixed up with the other, and that is a departure from reason. The theologian says: The substance in its self-subsistence is independent of a substrate in which it inheres such that the substrate is described by it; the accident in its need by its essence needs a substrate in which it inheres such that it is described by it. But it is not permissible for the substance to be stripped of all accidents, not because it needs them for its subsistence as a substance, but in its existence it is inconceivable that it be free from being in a particular place.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage contains the theologian's (mutakallim) response. First, he poses a question: why does the permissibility of the substance being free from one accident imply permissibility of being free from all accidents? He then presents an objection: if the substance could not be free from accidents, it would not be self-subsistent, which would blur the distinction between substance and accident. But the theologian then clarifies: the substance is self-subsistent in its essence, so it does not need accidents to be a substance. However, in its actual existence, it cannot be found without being in a particular place (an accident of 'where'). So it cannot be stripped of all accidents; it must have at least one accident of place. This resolves the confusion: the substance's subsistence is independent, but its existence requires some accident.

العرض
القيام بالذات
المحل
الكون في المكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the theologian agrees with the philosopher that substance can be stripped of all accidents.
Correction: The theologian clarifies that while substance does not need accidents for subsistence, in existence it must have at least the accident of place.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would then be incorporeal
B) Because it needs accidents for its subsistence
C) Because in its existence it must have a place
D) Because accidents are necessary for its definition

Key Takeaway
The substance does not need accidents for its subsistence but must have at least the accident of place in its existence.

Matter Must Have Position Before Receiving Form
The proponents of forms prove prime matter must have position to receive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that prime matter cannot be completely formless, because receiving form requires prior position and extension.

Core Idea
To receive any form, matter must already have position and extension.

The proponents of forms offer a clever thought experiment to show that prime matter cannot exist stripped of all forms. They imagine matter without any attributes, then consider what happens when magnitude is added. Their reasoning demonstrates that matter must always possess at least the form of extension, or a contradiction follows.

Guided Path
1
Set up the hypothetical
Imagine matter stripped of all forms, including position, place, dimension, and magnitude. Then consider adding magnitude to it.
This is a supposition to test the logical consequences.
2
Analyze the first case: sudden acquisition
If magnitude is acquired all at once, matter must already have a 'where' to receive it, meaning it already had position. But that contradicts the assumption of being formless.
The argument uses the idea that a thing cannot receive a property without some prior basis.
3
Analyze the second case: gradual acquisition
If magnitude is acquired gradually, matter must have directions and thus position. Again, this contradicts the initial assumption.
Gradual change implies spatial extension, which presupposes position.
4
Conclude the contradiction
Both possibilities lead to contradiction with the assumption of matter without position. Therefore, matter must always have some form (like extension) to receive any other form.
This reinforces the doctrine that prime matter never exists in a completely formless state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أصحاب الصور : لو قدرنا الهيولي جوهرا قائما بذاته عربا عن الصور كلها حتى لا يكون له وضع وحيز وبعد واتصال ومقدار يقبل الانقسام، ثم قدرناه حصل فيه المقدار مثلا ؛ فإما أن يصادفه المقدار دفعة أو على تدريج، فإن صادفه دفعة حتى حصل ذا مقدار فيكون قد صادفه بحيث ) فيكون الله حيث وحيز فيجب أن يكون ذا حيز أولا حتى يصادفه حيث هو فيكون ذا صورة، وإن صادفه على تدريج أو انبساط فكل ما من شأنه أن ينبسط فله جهات، وكل ما له جهة ) فهو ذو وضع، وعلى الوجهين جميعا لا يصادفه الاتصال والمقدار إلا أن يكون له حيث وحيز ووضع، وقد فرض غير متحيز وذا وضع فهو خلف.
And the proponents of forms said: If we suppose prime matter to be a self-subsistent substance stripped of all forms so that it has no position, place, dimension, connection, or magnitude receptive of division, then we suppose that magnitude comes to exist in it, for example. Then either magnitude meets it all at once or gradually. If it meets it all at once so that it becomes possessing magnitude, then it would have met it while having [no position], so it would have a where and a place, so it must have a place first so that it is met where it is, thus it would have a form. And if it meets it gradually or by expansion, then everything that is capable of expansion has directions, and everything that has direction has position. In both cases, connection and magnitude do not meet it unless it has a where, a place, and a position, but it was assumed to be non-extended and without position, so that is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from the proponents of forms (as'hab al-sura). They suppose prime matter stripped of all forms, including position, place, dimension, and magnitude. Then they consider the addition of magnitude to it. They argue that magnitude cannot be added to something that has no position or extension, because to receive magnitude, the matter must already have some extension or place to receive it. Whether it receives magnitude suddenly or gradually, in both cases it would require some prior position or direction. Since the initial assumption was that matter had no such attributes, this leads to a contradiction (khulf). Therefore, prime matter cannot exist without any form; it must always have at least the form of extension.

الصور
المقدار
الوضع
الحيز
الاتصال

Correction: This argument shows that matter must have at least the form of extension or position to receive any other form, so it cannot be completely formless.

Checkpoint

A) Prime matter can exist without any form
B) Prime matter must always have some form like extension
C) Magnitude cannot be added to matter
D) Matter has no position

Key Takeaway

The Contradiction of a Formless Substrate
A reductio argument against a formless substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why prime matter cannot exist without any form, as demonstrated through a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
Prime matter stripped of all forms leads to a logical contradiction.

The supporters of forms (as'hab al-suwar) propose a thought experiment: imagine prime matter (hayula) as a bare substratum with no attributes. Then consider introducing magnitude. This leads to a contradiction, proving that prime matter must always be united with some form. Let's follow their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Set up the thought experiment
We are asked to suppose that hayula is a self-subsistent substance completely stripped of all forms – no position, location, extension, contiguity, or magnitude.
This is the initial assumption to be tested.
2
Consider the two possibilities for receiving magnitude
When magnitude is introduced, it either happens all at once or gradually. Each possibility is examined.
3
Derive the contradiction in both cases
If all at once, the hayula must already have location to receive it. If gradually, it must have direction and position. Both contradict the assumption that it has none. Hence, the assumption is false.
This shows that hayula always coexists with form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أصحاب الصور : لو قدرنا الهيولي جوهرا قائما بذاته عربا عن الصور كلها حتى لا يكون له وضع وحيز وبعد واتصال ومقدار يقبل الانقسام، ثم قدرناه حصل فيه المقدار مثلا؛ فإما أن يصادفه المقدار دفعة أو على تدريج، فإن صادفه دفعة حتى حصل ذا مقدار فيكون قد صادفه بحيث يكون له حيث وحيز فيجب أن يكون ذا حيز أولا حتى يصادفه حيث هو فيكون ذا صورة، وإن صادفه على تدريج أو انبساط فكل ما من شأنه أن ينبسط فله جهات، وكل ما له جهة فهو ذو وضع، وعلى الوجهين جميعا لا يصادفه الاتصال والمقدار إلا أن يكون له حيث وحيز ووضع، وقد فرض غير متحيز وذا وضع فهو خلف. قال أصحاب الهيولي : كل حادث في زمان فيجب أن يسبقه إمكان الوجود، وذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون في نفس المقدر، وإما أن يكون في شيء، وبطل أن يكون في نفس المقدر، فإن المقدر لو قدر عدمه لم ينعدم الإمكان، فيتعين أنه في شيء ما خارج عن الذهن، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما، ومحال أن يكون معدوما؛ فإن المعدوم قبل والمعدوم مع شيء واحد، وليس الإمكان قبل هو الإمكان مع، وإن كان موجودا فإما أن يكون قائما في موضوع، وإما أن يكون قائما لا في موضوع فكل ما هو قائم لا في موضوع فله وجود خاص، لا يجب أن يكون به مضافا، وإمكان الوجود بما هو إمكان أمر مضاف لما هو إمكان له فهو إذا أمر في موضوع عارض لموضوع، ونحن نسميه قوة الوجود، والذي فيه قوة وجود الشيء والموضوع والمادة والهيولي قالوا: والإمكان قد يعري عن الوجود؛ لأنه لو تحقق وجود كل ممكن الحصلت موجودات بغير نهاية فالعالم قد سبقه إمكان الوجود، وقد سبقه الهيولي التي فيها الإمكان والإمكان لم يكن في الأزل فالهيولي لم تكن في الأزل، فتصور وجود العالم حيث تحقق الهيولي، وتحقق الهيولي حيث تحقق الإمكان، والإمكان والوجوب لا يجتمعان.
And the supporters of forms (as'hab al-suwar) said: If we suppose the prime matter (al-hayula) to be a substance subsisting by itself, stripped of all forms (suwar) so that it has no position (wad'), no location (hayyiz), no extension (bu'd), no contiguity (ittisal), and no magnitude (miqdar) that admits division, and then we suppose that magnitude occurs in it, for example; then either magnitude encounters it all at once or gradually. If it encounters it all at once so that it becomes possessed of magnitude, then it would have encountered it in such a way that it already has a where (hayth) and location, so it must have had location first so that it encounters it where it is, and thus it would be possessed of form. And if it encounters it gradually or by expansion, then everything that is apt to expand has directions (jihaat), and everything that has direction is possessed of position. On both sides, contiguity and magnitude do not encounter it unless it has where, location, and position, but it was assumed to be non-located and possessed of position, so that is a contradiction (khulf). The supporters of the prime matter (as'hab al-hayula) said: Every temporal event (hadith fi zaman) must be preceded by the possibility of existence (imkan al-wujud). That possibility is either in the thing itself that is estimated (al-muqaddar) or in something else. It is invalid that it be in the thing estimated, for if the estimated thing were supposed to not exist, the possibility would not cease to exist. So it is determined that it is in something outside the mind. That something is either existent or non-existent. It is impossible for it to be non-existent, for the non-existent prior and the non-existent with are one thing, and the possibility prior is not the possibility with. If it is existent, then it is either subsisting in a subject (qa'im fi mawdu') or subsisting not in a subject. Everything that subsists not in a subject has a particular existence (wujud khas) that need not be relative (mudaf). But the possibility of existence, qua possibility, is a relative matter (amr mudaf) to that which it is a possibility for. So it is a matter in a subject, accidental to the subject. And we call it power of existence (quwwat al-wujud). That in which is the power of existence of a thing is the subject (mawdu'), the matter (madda), and the prime matter (al-hayula). They said: And possibility may be divested of existence; for if the existence of every possible were realized, there would exist endless existents. So the world is preceded by the possibility of existence, and it is preceded by the prime matter in which is the possibility. And possibility was not in eternity (al-azal), so the prime matter was not in eternity. Thus the existence of the world is conceived when the prime matter is realized, and the prime matter is realized when possibility is realized. And possibility and necessity (al-wujub) do not coexist.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments from the supporters of hylomorphism (as'hab al-hayula, also called as'hab al-suwar). The first argument shows that prime matter (hayula) cannot exist stripped of all forms because a form like magnitude cannot be introduced into a completely unformed substrate without contradiction. If magnitude were to appear all at once, the substrate must already have location to receive it; if gradually, it must have direction and position. But the substrate was assumed to have none of these, leading to a contradiction (khulf). Therefore, prime matter always coexists with some form. The second argument proves the necessity of a substrate for potentiality (imkan al-wujud). Every temporal event must be preceded by possibility. This possibility cannot reside in the thing that will come to be, because that thing is non-existent, and possibility is distinct from non-existence. So it must be in something existent that serves as a subject (mawdu'). This subject is the power of existence (quwwat al-wujud), which is the prime matter (hayula). Since possibility is not eternal, the prime matter is also not eternal. Thus the world's existence is preceded by both possibility and prime matter, which are contingent. The passage concludes that possibility and necessity do not coexist, implying that only God is necessary.

Key Terms
الهيولي (al-hayula)
الصور (al-suwar)
الخلف (al-khulf)
الوضع (al-wad')
الحيز (al-hayyiz)
البعد (al-bu'd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prime matter can exist independently without any form.
Correction: The argument shows that a formless substrate would be unable to receive any form without contradiction, so prime matter must always be united with some form.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of assuming prime matter (hayula) is completely formless and then introducing magnitude?
A) Magnitude can be introduced without problem.
B) It leads to a contradiction because magnitude requires location or direction.
C) The hayula becomes the form itself.
D) Magnitude cannot be introduced at all.

Key Takeaway
Prime matter cannot be completely formless; it must always be associated with some form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that you cannot have a 'bare' matter without any form. In your own spiritual life, do you ever try to separate your faith from its outward forms—like prayer, charity, or fasting? How does this lesson challenge the idea that you can have a 'formless' spirituality?
?
The reductio forces you to confront a contradiction. What is one assumption you hold about your own character or soul that might be contradictory when examined closely? Are you willing to abandon that assumption?
?
If matter cannot exist without form, then your very existence is shaped by the forms you adopt. What forms (habits, thoughts, actions) are currently shaping you? Are they the forms you would choose, or are you passively receiving them? Commit to actively shaping one form today.

Possibility Requires a Substrate
An argument proving the necessity of a substrate for possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility of existence (imkan al-wujud) must inhere in a real subject, which is prime matter.

Core Idea
Possibility is a real attribute that must inhere in a subject.

The supporters of prime matter (as'hab al-hayula) present a second argument: every temporal event is preceded by possibility. This possibility cannot reside in the event itself (as it is non-existent) nor in nothing, so it must be in an existent subject. That subject is prime matter, the bearer of potentiality. Let's examine the reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Premise: Every temporal event has possibility
The argument begins by asserting that every temporal event (hadith fi zaman) is preceded by the possibility of existence (imkan al-wujud).
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
2
Locate the possibility
The possibility must either be in the thing itself (the event that will occur) or in something else. It cannot be in the thing itself because if the thing were non-existent, the possibility would remain, indicating it is distinct.
This shows possibility is not identical to the thing.
3
The possibility is in an existent subject
The possibility must be in something external to the mind, and that something must be existent (not non-existent). Moreover, that existent must be a subject (mawdu') that bears the possibility as an accident.
The argument eliminates non-existence as a candidate.
4
Identify the subject as prime matter
The subject that bears possibility is called the 'power of existence' (quwwat al-wujud), which is also the matter (madda) and prime matter (hayula). This subject is not eternal, as possibility is not eternal.
Thus prime matter is the substrate for all change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
If possibility is a real attribute that inheres in you, what does that imply about the responsibility you have to actualize that potential? Are you leaving possibilities unrealized and thus failing to honor the reality of your own existence?
?
The passage says possibility may be divested of existence – not every possible becomes actual. In light of this, consider: are you using your finite time to bring into existence the possibilities that matter most, or are you letting them remain merely possible?
