The Materialist Proof for the Necessity of a Substrate of Potentiality
Materialist proof that possibility requires a substrate (matter).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist argument that every temporal event requires a preceding potentiality that must inhere in a subject (matter), and why it cannot be in the thing itself or in non-existence.

Core Idea
Potentiality cannot float freely—it must rest in a substrate of matter.

The materialists (ashab al-hayula) present a rational proof for the existence of matter as the substrate of potentiality. This lesson walks through their argument that every temporal event is preceded by possibility (imkan), and that possibility must be an accident inhering in a pre-existent subject. Let's follow their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting premise
Read the first sentence carefully: every temporal event is preceded by the possibility of existence (imkan al-wujud). This is the foundation of the argument.
2
Why possibility cannot be in the thing itself
The argument eliminates the idea that possibility is inherent in the estimated thing itself. If you imagine the thing non-existent, the possibility remains, so it must be elsewhere.
3
Understanding the substrate must be existent
The possibility must be in something outside the mind. That something cannot be non-existent, because non-existence is uniform but possibility changes. So it must be existent.
4
Possibility is a relational accident, hence matter
Since possibility is a relational attribute (amr mudaf), it cannot be a substance. Therefore it must be an accident inhering in a subject, which they call matter or hyle.
This step concludes the core proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب الهيولي: كل حادث في زمان فيجب أن يسبقه إمكان الوجود، وذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون في نفس المقدر، وإما أن يكون في شيء، وبطل أن يكون في نفس المقدر، فإن المقدر لو قدر عدمه لم ينعدم الإمكان، فيتعين أنه في شيء ما خارج عن الذهن، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما، ومحال أن يكون معدوما؛ فإن المعدوم قبل والمعدوم مع شيء واحد، وليس الإمكان قبل هو الإمكان مع، وإن كان موجودا فإما أن يكون قائما في موضوع، وإما أن يكون قائما لا في موضوع فكل ما هو قائم لا في موضوع فله وجود خاص، لا يجب أن يكون به مضافا، وإمكان الوجود بما هو إمكان أمر مضاف لما هو إمكان له فهو إذا أمر في موضوع عارض لموضوع، ونحن نسميه قوة الوجود، والذي فيه قوة وجود الشيء والموضوع والمادة والهيولي قالوا: والإمكان قد يعري عن الوجود؛ لأنه لو تحقق وجود كل ممكن الحصلت موجودات بغير نهاية فالعالم قد سبقه إمكان الوجود، وقد سبقه الهيولي التي فيها الإمكان والإمكان لم يكن في الأزل فالهيولي لم تكن في الأزل، فتصور وجود العالم حيث تحقق الهيولي، وتحقق الهيولي حيث تحقق الإمكان، والإمكان والوجوب لا يجتمعان.
The proponents of matter said: Every temporal event must be preceded by the possibility of existence. That possibility is either in the estimated thing itself or in something else. It is false that it is in the estimated thing itself, because if one estimates its non-existence, the possibility does not cease to exist. So it is determined that it is in something outside the mind. That something is either existent or non-existent. It cannot be non-existent, for non-existence before and non-existence after are one thing, and the possibility before is not the possibility after. And if it is existent, then it is either subsistent in a subject or subsistent not in a subject. Everything that is subsistent not in a subject has a particular existence that does not have to be relational. And possibility of existence, qua possibility, is a relational attribute to that for which it is a possibility. Therefore, it is something in a subject, accidental to a subject, and we call it the potency of existence. That in which is the potency of existence of something is the subject, matter, and hyle. They said: Possibility may be stripped of existence, because if the existence of every possible thing were realized, existents without end would occur. So the world was preceded by the possibility of existence and was preceded by the matter which contains that possibility. Possibility was not in pre-eternity, so matter was not in pre-eternity. Thus the existence of the world is conceived where matter is realized, and matter is realized where possibility is realized, and possibility and necessity do not coexist.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the materialists (ashab al-hayula) that every temporal event requires a preceding possibility (imkan) which must inhere in a substrate that is existent, leading to the postulation of matter (hayula) as the subject of potentiality. The argument proceeds step by step: First, every temporal event is preceded by the possibility of its existence. This possibility cannot be in the estimated thing itself, because if we imagine the thing does not exist, the possibility still exists. So it must be in something outside the mind. That something must be existent, because non-existence is uniform and cannot be a substrate for a relational attribute like possibility. If it is existent, it is either a substance (qaim bi nafsihi) or an accident (qaim fi mawdu). Possibility is a relational attribute, so it cannot be a substance but must be an accident inhering in a subject. They call this subject 'potency of existence' (quwwat al-wujud), and the subject itself is called matter or hyle. They further argue that possibility can be stripped from actual existence, leading to a potential infinite regress of existents. Therefore, the world was preceded by possibility and matter, and since possibility is not eternal, matter is not eternal. Thus the world is conceived as generated when matter and possibility are realized, and possibility and necessity (wujub) cannot coexist.

Key Terms
إمكان
هيولي
قوة الوجود
مادة
موضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an intrinsic property of the thing itself, needing no separate substrate.
Correction: The argument shows that possibility remains even if the thing is absent, so it cannot be inherent in the thing; it requires a separate subject.

Checkpoint
According to the materialists, why cannot the possibility of existence be in the estimated thing itself?
A) Because the estimated thing is non-existent.
B) Because the possibility would cease when the thing is estimated as non-existent.
C) Because possibility is relational and cannot be self-subsistent.
D) Because the estimated thing is not a subject.

Key Takeaway
The materialist proof for matter hinges on the insight that possibility is a relational accident requiring a substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very ability to act and change is not self-sufficient but depends on a substrate given by Allah, does that change how you view your daily choices? Are you taking your potential for granted or recognizing it as a constant gift that needs nurturing?
?
The argument shows that change requires a substrate that remains. What is the one unchanging foundation in your life that all your changes rest upon? How does today's lesson challenge you to strengthen that foundation rather than just focusing on surface changes?
?
The materialists locate the substrate in matter. But as a believer, your ultimate substrate is Allah's sustaining will. When you feel your own potential is weak or scattered, do you turn to the One who holds all existence together, or do you try to ground yourself in something less stable?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Implications: Possibility Stripped of Existence and the Temporality of Matter
Further materialist reasoning on possibility without existence and the temporality of matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist argument that possibility can be separated from actual existence, leading to the conclusion that matter is not eternal but temporally preceded the world.

Core Idea
Possibility without existence shows that matter must be temporal, not eternal.

After establishing that possibility inheres in matter, the materialists extend the argument to show that possibility can exist without actuality. This leads to a potential infinite regress of existents and forces the conclusion that matter itself is not eternal. Let's see how this reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the stripping claim
The materialists assert that possibility may be stripped of existence (imkan qad ya'ra 'an al-wujud). This means potentiality does not require actuality.
2
Consider the consequence of realized possibility
If every possible thing were actualized, there would be an infinite number of existents. This would be absurd, so not all possibilities are realized.
3
Apply to the world's origin
The world's existence was preceded by possibility and matter. Since possibility is not eternal, matter is not eternal either.
4
Conclusion about coexistence
Possibility and necessity (wujub) cannot coexist. Therefore, when matter and possibility are realized, necessity is absent, implying the world is contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب الهيولي: كل حادث في زمان فيجب أن يسبقه إمكان الوجود، وذلك الإمكان إما أن يكون في نفس المقدر، وإما أن يكون في شيء، وبطل أن يكون في نفس المقدر، فإن المقدر لو قدر عدمه لم ينعدم الإمكان، فيتعين أنه في شيء ما خارج عن الذهن، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما، ومحال أن يكون معدوما؛ فإن المعدوم قبل والمعدوم مع شيء واحد، وليس الإمكان قبل هو الإمكان مع، وإن كان موجودا فإما أن يكون قائما في موضوع، وإما أن يكون قائما لا في موضوع فكل ما هو قائم لا في موضوع فله وجود خاص، لا يجب أن يكون به مضافا، وإمكان الوجود بما هو إمكان أمر مضاف لما هو إمكان له فهو إذا أمر في موضوع عارض لموضوع، ونحن نسميه قوة الوجود، والذي فيه قوة وجود الشيء والموضوع والمادة والهيولي قالوا: والإمكان قد يعري عن الوجود؛ لأنه لو تحقق وجود كل ممكن الحصلت موجودات بغير نهاية فالعالم قد سبقه إمكان الوجود، وقد سبقه الهيولي التي فيها الإمكان والإمكان لم يكن في الأزل فالهيولي لم تكن في الأزل، فتصور وجود العالم حيث تحقق الهيولي، وتحقق الهيولي حيث تحقق الإمكان، والإمكان والوجوب لا يجتمعان.
The proponents of matter said: Every temporal event must be preceded by the possibility of existence. That possibility is either in the estimated thing itself or in something else. It is false that it is in the estimated thing itself, because if one estimates its non-existence, the possibility does not cease to exist. So it is determined that it is in something outside the mind. That something is either existent or non-existent. It cannot be non-existent, for non-existence before and non-existence after are one thing, and the possibility before is not the possibility after. And if it is existent, then it is either subsistent in a subject or subsistent not in a subject. Everything that is subsistent not in a subject has a particular existence that does not have to be relational. And possibility of existence, qua possibility, is a relational attribute to that for which it is a possibility. Therefore, it is something in a subject, accidental to a subject, and we call it the potency of existence. That in which is the potency of existence of something is the subject, matter, and hyle. They said: Possibility may be stripped of existence, because if the existence of every possible thing were realized, existents without end would occur. So the world was preceded by the possibility of existence and was preceded by the matter which contains that possibility. Possibility was not in pre-eternity, so matter was not in pre-eternity. Thus the existence of the world is conceived where matter is realized, and matter is realized where possibility is realized, and possibility and necessity do not coexist.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the materialists (ashab al-hayula) that every temporal event requires a preceding possibility (imkan) which must inhere in a substrate that is existent, leading to the postulation of matter (hayula) as the subject of potentiality. The argument proceeds step by step: First, every temporal event is preceded by the possibility of its existence. This possibility cannot be in the estimated thing itself, because if we imagine the thing does not exist, the possibility still exists. So it must be in something outside the mind. That something must be existent, because non-existence is uniform and cannot be a substrate for a relational attribute like possibility. If it is existent, it is either a substance (qaim bi nafsihi) or an accident (qaim fi mawdu). Possibility is a relational attribute, so it cannot be a substance but must be an accident inhering in a subject. They call this subject 'potency of existence' (quwwat al-wujud), and the subject itself is called matter or hyle. They further argue that possibility can be stripped from actual existence, leading to a potential infinite regress of existents. Therefore, the world was preceded by possibility and matter, and since possibility is not eternal, matter is not eternal. Thus the world is conceived as generated when matter and possibility are realized, and possibility and necessity (wujub) cannot coexist.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Matter is eternal and coeternal with God or with possibility itself.
Correction: The argument shows that possibility is not eternal, so matter (its substrate) is also not eternal. Matter came into being along with possibility.

Checkpoint

B) Because if every possible thing existed, there would be an infinite number of existents.

D) Because matter is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Because possibility is not eternal, the matter that houses it cannot be eternal, implying the world's temporal origin.

?

Plato's Proof and the Inseparability of Matter and Form
Plato's proof and the formists' complement on inseparability of matter and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Plato's proof for the temporal origination of the world using form and matter is accepted and refined by the formists, who argue that matter and form are inseparable.

Core Idea
Matter and form are interdependent twins: one cannot exist without the other.

This lesson introduces a philosophical proof attributed to Plato for the world's temporal origination, using the concepts of form (ṣūra) and matter (hayūlā). The formists (aṣḥāb al-ṣuwar) accept the proof but add a crucial qualification: matter and form are never separate. This discussion deepens our understanding of how possibility (imkān) relates to material existence.

Guided Path
1
Read Plato's proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Plato's analogy between mental universals and external realities. Note that he argues for the existence of universal existents outside the mind, which differ by properties.
Plato's proof is concise and foundational here.
2
Understand the formists' acceptance and addition
The formists affirm Plato's proof for temporal existents but add that possibility (imkān) is only realized in matter, and matter is only conceivable with form. They explain why matter cannot be formless.
The key phrase: 'if it were stripped of all forms, the intention of the agent would not be realized.'
3
Trace the interdependence chain
Follow the logical sequence: possibility depends on matter, matter depends on form, form depends on the Bestower of Forms. Notice that nothing in this chain can be separated.
This highlights the createdness and contingency of the physical world.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that matter and form are never separate. Consider why this matters for proving temporal origination: it shows that the world always requires a creator (Bestower of Forms).
This refines the proof by emphasizing the inherent link between possibility and composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا دليل أفلاطن إلهي في حدوث العالم بصورته وهيولاه، قال: وكما تصور وجود موجودات كلية في العقل تصور وجود موجودات كلية في الخارج عن العقل، وهي حقائق مختلفة تختلف بالخواص كالعقول السماوية. وقال أصحاب الصور هذا البرهان صحيح في الموجودات الزمانية، لكن كما لا يتحقق إمكان إلا في مادة لا تتصور مادة إلا متصورة بصورة، فإنها إن كانت عرية عن الصور كلها كان لا يتحقق نحوها قصد الفاعل حتى يفيض عليها الصور؛ إذ لا حيث لها ولا وضع ولا أين وكما أن الإمكان ليس شيئا متقوما إلا بالهيولي، والهيولي ليس شيئا متقوما إلا بالصورة، فيقوم الهيولي بالصورة، وتقوم الصورة بواهب الصورة، وليس تنفك إحداهما عن الثانية بحال أصلا.
This is the proof of Plato the divine concerning the temporal origination of the world according to its form and matter. He said: Just as the existence of universal existents is conceived in the intellect, so the existence of universal existents is conceived outside the intellect, and they are different realities differing by properties like the celestial intellects. And the proponents of forms said: This proof is correct for temporal existents, but just as possibility is not realized except in matter, and matter is not conceived except as conjoined with form, for if it were stripped of all forms, the intention of the agent towards it would not be realized so as to bestow forms upon it, since it has no dimension, position, or place. And just as possibility is not self-subsistent except through matter, and matter is not self-subsistent except through form, so matter is subsistent through form, and form is subsistent through the bestower of form, and neither is ever separated from the other at all.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage first presents Plato's proof for the temporal origination of the world with form and matter. Plato analogizes: just as universal existents (like species) are conceived in the mind as general realities, so they exist outside the mind as distinct realities with specific properties, such as the celestial intellects. This suggests a dual aspect: universal form and individual matter. Then the formists (ashab al-surat) respond. They affirm the validity of Plato's proof for temporal existents but add an important qualification: possibility (imkan) is only realized in matter, and matter is only conceivable with form. If matter were completely stripped of form, it would have no dimension, position, or place, and thus no intention of the agent (fai') could be directed towards it to bestow form. Therefore, matter and form are mutually dependent: possibility subsists through matter, and matter subsists through form; form subsists through the bestower of form (wahib al-surat). They are never separate. The formists thus refine the materialist view by emphasizing the necessary conjunction of matter and form.

Key Terms

أصحاب الصور
صورة
هيولي
واهب الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the formists reject Plato's proof altogether.
Correction: The formists actually accept the proof for temporal existents but add the crucial condition that matter and form are inseparable, thereby deepening rather than rejecting the proof.

Checkpoint

D) Because it would be many.

Key Takeaway
Plato's proof for the world's temporal origination is valid, but it must be understood that matter and form are inseparable, each dependent on the other and ultimately on the Bestower of Forms.

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Self-Subsistent Matter
A dialectical proof showing matter's dependence on form through an infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument that matter cannot be self-subsistent because it would require another substrate to receive its forms, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If matter were self-subsistent, its change would imply a further substrate, causing infinite regress.

This passage presents another proof against the self-subsistence of matter (hayula). The argument uses a dichotomy: if matter is self-subsistent, it must be either one or many. By examining both possibilities, the proof shows that each leads to a contradiction or an infinite regress, demonstrating that matter always depends on form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial assumption and dichotomy
Assume matter is self-subsistent. Ask: Is it one or many? If one, how does it become two? This frames the argument.
The argument is dialectical, starting from a common assumption.
2
Explore the first possibility: addition from outside
If matter becomes two by adding another matter, then two substances join, and the duality implies form (either separation or number). So matter requires form.
3
Explore the second possibility: self-multiplication
If matter multiplies itself without addition, it changes from indivisible to divisible. It thus has forms of unity and multiplicity at different times, requiring a common substrate that receives both forms. That substrate would be another matter, leading to infinite regress.
This is the key step showing regress.
4
Conclude that matter always depends on form
Both possibilities show that matter cannot be self-subsistent without form. The proof establishes that matter is always conjoined with form.
The conclusion is that matter is not self-subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان آخر لو قدرنا الهيولي موجودة متقومة، فإما أن يقال: هي واحدة أو كثيرة، فإن كانت واحدة ثم صارت اثنين، أفبانضمام آخر إليه أم بتكثير ذلك الواحد في نفسه من غير انضمام من خارج؟ فإن قدر الأول فهما جوهران انضم أحدهما إلى الثاني، ويكون الهيولي اثنين وما تحقق فيه الاثنينية قبل القسمة إما بالانفصال إذا كانا متصلين وإما بالعدد إذا كان مقدارين وكل ذلك صورة، وإن تكثر الواحد في نفسه من غير انضمام من خارج كان حين كان واحدا لم يقبل القسمة ثم صار قابلا للقسمة فتكون له صورة الوحدة تارة، وصورة التكثر تارة، فيكون ذا صورتين، ويلزم أن يكون بين الحالتين مادة مشتركة بها يقبل الوحدة تارة والكثرة أخرى، فيكون للمادة مادة ويتسلسل.
Another proof: If we suppose matter is existent and self-subsistent, then it must be either one or many. If it is one and then becomes two, is it by the addition of another to it or by the multiplication of that one in itself without addition from outside? If the first is supposed, then there are two substances, one of which joined the other, so matter becomes two, and duality is realized in it before division, either by separation if they were connected or by number if they were quantities, and all that is form. If the one multiplies in itself without addition from outside, then when it was one it did not accept division, then it became capable of division, so it has a form of unity at one time and a form of multiplicity at another, thus having two forms, and it follows that between the two states there is a common substrate by which it accepts unity at one time and multiplicity at another, so matter would have matter, leading to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents another proof against the self-subsistence of matter (hayula). The argument is dialectical: assume matter is self-subsistent and ask whether it is one or many. If it is one and then becomes two, there are two possibilities: either by addition of another matter, or by self-multiplication. In the first case, two substances join, and matter becomes two. The duality is realized either by separation (if they were connected) or by number (if they are quantities). In both cases, this duality is a form, meaning matter requires form to become two. In the second case (self-multiplication), when matter was one it was indivisible, then became divisible, so it possesses the form of unity at one time and the form of multiplicity at another. This implies that matter has two successive forms, which requires a common substrate that receives both forms. That common substrate would be another matter, leading to an infinite regress of matter within matter. Thus, matter cannot be self-subsistent without form. The conclusion is that matter is always conjoined with form.

واحدة
كثيرة

تكثير
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The argument demonstrates that change in matter always involves form and leads to regress if matter is self-subsistent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that matter cannot be self-subsistent because any change in it implies form, and self-multiplication leads to an infinite regress of substrates.

Refuting Cause by Secondary Intent
Author argues cause cannot be by secondary intent without primary intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why something cannot be a cause or principle by secondary intent unless it is a cause or principle by primary intent, and how this refutes the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Secondary intent depends on primary intent; without essence, accident is impossible.

This passage presents a concise logical refutation of the claim that Allah is a cause only by secondary intent. The author establishes two conditional statements that show the necessary dependence of accidental causality on essential causality. By the end, we see that calling Allah a 'cause' in any meaningful sense requires essential causation, which the opponent denies, invalidating their attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic source and its literal translation. Notice the parallel structure of the two conditional statements.
Pay attention to the key terms: cause, principle, essential, accidental, first intent, second intent.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author uses two 'if not... then not' propositions. Identify each premise and how they are connected to the conclusion.
3
Understand the refutation
Consider why the opponent's claim fails. If something is not an essential cause, it cannot be an accidental cause. The same applies to first and second intent. Thus, the opponent cannot legitimately call Allah a cause.
This shows the dependency of secondary on primary.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the key point. Then, try the activity to articulate the argument in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : فما لم تكن العلة علة لشيء بالذات لا تكون علة لشيء آخر بالعرض، وما لم يكن مبدأ الشيء بالقصد الأول لا يكون مبدأ الشيء بالقصد الثاني، فيلزم ما لزم في الأول فيبطل إطلاق لفظ العلة والمبدأ عليه.
We say: For whatever is not a cause of something essentially, it is not a cause of another thing accidentally. And whatever is not a principle of a thing by first intent, it is not a principle of the thing by second intent. Thus what was necessary in the first case follows, and so the use of the words 'cause' and 'principle' for it is invalidated.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's claim that the cause is not by essence but by secondary intent. The author presents a conditional argument: if something is not an essential cause of anything, then it cannot be an accidental cause of something else. Similarly, if it is not a principle by first intent, it cannot be a principle by second intent. Therefore, the conclusion from the first part (that cause and principle are not applicable) applies also to the second, making the opponent's attribution of cause and principle to God invalid.

Key Terms

مبدأ
بالذات
بالعرض
القصد الأول
القصد الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that secondary intent alone suffices for something to be a cause or principle, without any primary intent.
Correction: This passage shows that secondary intent depends on primary intent; without primary intent, secondary intent is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) It cannot be a cause by accident.
C) It is a cause by secondary intent.
D) It is a cause by first intent.

Key Takeaway
Without essential causality, accidental causality is impossible, thus attributing cause or principle to Allah by secondary intent alone is invalid.

Why Nearness and Distance Don't Apply to Divine Causation
Author refutes the analogy of nearness/distance for causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy of nearness and distance cannot be used to argue about divine causation.

Core Idea
Nearness and distance involve multiple categories, refuting the analogy for causation.

In this passage, the author tackles an opponent's attempt to draw an analogy between divine causation and physical nearness/distance. The opponent claimed that just as nearness is not an additional attribute to a substance, cause is not additional to God's essence. But the author reveals that nearness and distance are far from simple—they involve different categories and lead to absurd consequences if treated as the opponent assumes. Let's see how the author dismantles this analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get the flow. Notice the technical terms: قرب, بعد, إضافة, وضع, أين.
2
Understand the opponent's analogy
The opponent used the analogy of nearness/distance to suggest that causation is not an additional attribute. Grasp why before seeing the refutation.
3
Identify the key categories
The author lists three categories under which nearness/distance can fall: relation, position, location. Recognize that nearness/distance are not a single simple thing.
4
Follow the logical consequence
If nearness/distance were additional accidents, they would be infinite for finite substances—an absurdity. Note how the author uses this to turn the analogy against the opponent.
5
Conclude the refutation
The analogy actually supports the opposite view: that the relevant categories are finite and additional, so causing is likely additional too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخالف ما نحن فيه حال القرب والبعد بالإضافة إلى شيء دون شيء، فإن قرب الجوهر من جوهر قد يكون من باب الإضافة، وقد يكون من باب الوضع، وقد يكون من باب الأين، لكن لفظ القرب والبعد يطلق على كل جوهرين تقاربًا أو تباعدًا بمقدار ما، والمقادير بينهما لا تنحصر في حد، فقيل: فعند ذلك تختلف بالنسب والإضافات وهي لا تنحصر فلو قلنا: إنهما أمران زائدان على وجودهما أدى ذلك إلى إثبات أعراض لا تتناهى لجوهرين متناهيين لكن ما فيه القرب والبعد متناهيين؛ أعني: الأين والوضع والإضافة، فهي أمور زائدة على الجوهرين، فعرف أن المثال الذي تمثلوا به لازم عليهم.
And the case of nearness and distance in relation to one thing and not another differs from what we are discussing. For the nearness of a substance to a substance may be of the category of relation, or of position, or of location. But the term 'nearness' and 'distance' is applied to every two substances that are near or far to some extent, and the measures between them are not limited to a limit. So it is said: Then they differ according to relations and additions, and they are unlimited. So if we said that they are two matters additional to their existence, that would lead to establishing an infinite number of accidents for two finite substances. But that in which nearness and distance exist are finite—namely: location, position, and relation—for they are matters additional to the two substances. Thus it is known that the example they used as an analogy is binding against them.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's analogy between divine causation and the relation of nearness and distance. The author points out that nearness and distance are complex: they can fall under different categories (relation, position, location) and are applied to substances in varying degrees. If we considered them as additional accidents, that would lead to an infinite regress of accidents for finite substances, which is absurd. The categories involved (location, position, relation) are themselves finite and additional. Therefore, the analogy does not support the opponent's claim that cause is not additional to essence; rather, it shows the opposite, that the example actually works against them.

Key Terms
قرب
بعد

Correction: They involve multiple categories (relation, position, location) and lead to infinite regress, so the analogy fails.

Paternity vs. Causality: A Logical Objection
Author demands a distinction between paternity and causality as relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that if paternity is an accidental relation, then causality must also be an accidental relation, and why this leads to an unacceptable conclusion for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Relations are all alike: if one is an accident, all are accidents.

The author challenges his opponents to explain why they treat paternity (a relation) as an accident but refuse to treat causality (also a relation) the same way. If both are pure relations, consistency demands the same ruling. The absurd result: God's essential necessitation would become a kind of reproduction, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the original objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the rhetorical question structure: 'Did you not affirm... Then inform us of a distinction...'
The author is using the opponent's own premise against them.
2
Understand the two relations
Identify the two examples: the 'father-son' relation (paternity) and the 'cause-effect' relation (causality). Both are defined as 'having existence only in relation to another'.
3
Trace the logical move
See how the author argues that both relations share the same definition. If paternity is an accident, then causality must also be an accident. The opponent is inconsistent.
4
Grasp the absurd consequence
If causality were an accident in God, then God's essential necessitation (creating the world) would become a kind of reproduction (viladah), which is absurd for the Creator.
This shows why the opponent's position fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نقول : ألستم أثبتم الإضافة معنى وعرضا زائدا على الجوهر؟ فأفيدونا فرقا معقولا بين إضافة الأب إلى الابن وبين إضافة العلة إلى المعلول، فإن الإضافة هو المعنى الذي وجوده بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، وليس له وجود غيره مثل الأبوة بالقياس إلى البنوة لا كالأب فإن له وجودا كالإنسانية، وكونه مصدرا ومبدأ هو المعنى الذي وجوده بالقياس إلى المعلول وليس له وجود غيره، وكذلك كل علة ومعلول سوى العلة الأولى فتعرض له هذا المعنى لو هو هذا المعنى بعينه، ثم حكمتم بأن الأبوة معنى هو عرض زائد فهلا حكمتم بأن العلية معنى هو عرض زائد حتى يلزم أن يكون الإيجاب بالذات نوع ولادة؟
Moreover, we say: Did you not affirm that relation is a meaning and an accident additional to the substance? Then inform us of a reasonable distinction between the relation of father to son and the relation of cause to effect. For the relation is the meaning whose existence is in relation to something else, and it has no existence other than that, like paternity in relation to sonship, not like the father—for he has an existence like humanity. And His being a source and a principle is the meaning whose existence is in relation to the effect and has no existence other than that. Similarly, every cause and effect other than the First Cause, this meaning occurs to it—or rather it is this very meaning. Then you ruled that paternity is a meaning that is an additional accident, so why do you not rule that causality is a meaning that is an additional accident, such that it would entail that essential necessitation is a kind of reproduction?
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an objection that presses the opponent to apply the same logic to causality as they do to paternity. The author notes that the opponent considers paternity (relation) to be an accident additional to the substance. Then they argue that causality is also a relational meaning: it exists only in relation to the effect, just like paternity exists only in relation to sonship. If paternity is an added accident, then causality should also be an added accident. The absurd consequence is that essential necessitation ('إيجاب بالذات') would then become a kind of reproduction (ولادة), which is clearly unacceptable for God. Therefore, the opponent's position is inconsistent.

عرض

أبوة

علة
معلول
إيجاب بالذات
ولادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causality is not a relation; it is a real attribute inherent in the cause.
Correction: The author defines causality as a pure relation: its existence is only in relation to the effect, just like paternity exists only in relation to sonship.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author draw if causality were an added accident like paternity?
A) Causality becomes impossible.
B) Essential necessitation becomes a kind of reproduction.
C) God would have multiple accidents.
D) Nothing changes.

Key Takeaway
The author forces a consistency check: if paternity is an accidental relation, causality must be too, leading to the absurdity that God's creative act becomes a form of reproduction.

The author shows that calling paternity an accident forces calling causality an accident. Where in your own beliefs about God do you hold two positions that, if examined, lead to an absurd conclusion? Will you honestly trace the logic today?

When you hear a claim about God that seems reasonable, do you test it against His transcendence, or do you accept it comfortably? The Argument about Relation between Father-Son and Cause-Effect
The author challenges philosophers to distinguish father-son and cause-effect relations, arguing both are accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concept of relation to argue that causality, like fatherhood, is an accidental relation, thereby refuting the philosophers' notion of necessary emanation.

Core Idea
Causality and fatherhood are both accidental relations; denying one makes the other absurd.

In this passage, the author directly challenges the philosophers who accept that fatherhood is an accidental relation. He asks them to distinguish it from causality, arguing that both are relations that exist only by reference to something else. If they insist that fatherhood is an accident, they must also treat causality as an accident, which would make God's necessary emanation a kind of generation—an unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage aloud, then the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical tone: the author is challenging the philosophers.
The passage starts with 'على أنا نقول' which signals a direct objection.
2
Identify the core challenge
The author asks for a 'reasonable distinction' between father-son and cause-effect relations. Underline this request.
3
Understand the definition of relation
The author defines relation as a meaning that exists only by reference to something else. He gives examples: fatherhood exists only by reference to sonship; the father himself has independent existence (humanity).
This definition is crucial for the argument.
4
Apply the definition to causality
The author says the same applies to causality: being a cause (source/principe) exists only by reference to the effect. For every cause except the First Cause, this relation occurs.
The exception of the First Cause is important—it sets up the reductio.
5
Follow the reductio conclusion
Since the philosophers consider fatherhood an accident, they must also consider causality an accident. If so, then God's necessary emanation would be a kind of generation (wilada), which is absurd.
This shows the inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نقول : ألستم أثبتم الإضافة معنى وعرضا زائدا على الجوهر؟ فأفيدونا فرقا معقولا بين إضافة الأب إلى الابن وبين إضافة العلة إلى المعلول، فإن الإضافة هو المعنى الذي وجوده بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، وليس له وجود غيره مثل الأبوة بالقياس إلى البنوة لا كالأب فإن له وجودًا كالإنسانية، وكونه مصدرًا ومبدأ هو المعنى الذي وجوده بالقياس إلى المعلول وليس له وجود غيره، وكذلك كل علة ومعلول سوى العلة الأولى فتعرض لهذا المعنى لو هو هذا المعنى بعينه، ثم حكمتم بأن الأبوة معنى هو عرض زائد فهلا حكمتم بأن العلية معنى هو عرض زائد حتى يلزم أن يكون الإيجاب بالذات نوع ولادة؟
Moreover, we say: Did you not establish that relation (al-idafah) is a meaning and an accident additional to the substance? So inform us of a reasonable distinction between the relation of the father to the son and the relation of the cause to the effect. For relation is the meaning whose existence is by reference to something else, and it has no other existence, like fatherhood in reference to sonship, not like the father, for he has existence like humanity. And that He is a source and principle is the meaning whose existence is by reference to the effect and has no other existence. And likewise every cause and effect other than the First Cause, then this meaning occurs to it if it is this same meaning. Then you ruled that fatherhood is a meaning that is an additional accident. So why did you not rule that causality is a meaning that is an additional accident, so that it would necessitate that the necessary emanation (al-ijab bi al-dhat) is a kind of generation (wilada)?
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a challenge and a reductio ad absurdum. The author addresses those (philosophers) who assert that relation (al-idafah) is an accident added to the substance. He asks them to provide a reasonable distinction between the relation of father to son and the relation of cause to effect. He defines relation as a meaning that exists only by reference to something else, like fatherhood exists only by reference to sonship, whereas the father himself has an independent existence (humanity). Similarly, being a source and principle (mabda') exists only by reference to the effect. He then points out that for every cause and effect except the First Cause, this meaning (relation) occurs to it, and it is the same meaning. Therefore, if they consider fatherhood an additional accident, they should also consider causality an additional accident. If they do, then the necessary emanation (al-ijab bi al-dhat) of the First Cause would be a kind of generation (wilada), which is absurd and blasphemous.

Key Terms
الإضافة
العلة والمعلول
الإيجاب بالذات
ولادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that causality is not a relation or that it is a different kind of relation from fatherhood.
Correction: The author argues that causality is exactly the same kind of relation as fatherhood: both exist only by reference to something else. Therefore, if fatherhood is an accident, so is causality.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the philosophers' acceptance that fatherhood is an additional accident?
A) Causality is also an additional accident.
B) Causality is not an accident.
C) The First Cause is not a cause.
D) Fathers and causes are the same.

Key Takeaway
The author uses the concept of relation as an accident to show that causality, like fatherhood, is accidental, thereby refuting the philosophers' concept of necessary emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Sifatiyya's Redefinition of Causation
The Sifatiyya object to restricting causation to the contingent by redefining causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's objection to restricting causation to contingent things and their redefinition of causation as intellectual entailment.

Core Idea
The Sifatiyya argue that causation does not mean creation but logical entailment.

The author sets forth a key theological principle: only contingent things need causes, while the Necessary Being is self-sufficient. However, the Sifatiyya challenge this by offering a different definition of causation—one that does not require contingency. In this passage, you will see the principle stated and then immediately contested.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the author's initial principle
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author argues that the necessary being does not need a cause, while contingent things do.
Pay attention to the example of knowledge in the witnessed world.
2
Focus on the Sifatiyya's objection
The Sifatiyya object by redefining what it means for something to be 'caused'. They distinguish between causation as creation and causation as logical entailment.
They use the terms 'intellectual entailment' and 'real correlation'.
3
Understand their redefinition
The Sifatiyya claim that neediness does not necessitate causation, and independence does not prevent it, because they define causation differently.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this redefinition affects the original principle. If causation is logical entailment, then necessity does not preclude being caused.
This is a key point of debate in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك، وخرجوا عن هذه القاعدة كونه مريدًا، فإنه لما لم يكن واجب كان معللا، وهذا كله لأن الواجب يستقل بوجوبه عن الافتقار إلى العلة، وإنما الجائز لما لم يستقل بنفسه؛ أعني أحد طرفي جوازه احتاج إلى علة، إما اختيار مختار وإما إيجاب علة، فوجود العلم في العالم شاهدًا لما كان جائزا احتاج إلى اختيار مختار يوجده، وثبوت حكم العلة في الشاهد لما كان جائزا احتاج إلى علة توجبه وهي العلم، أليس وجود القديم لما كان واجبًا لم يعلل ووجود الحادث لما كان جائزا علل؟ قالت الصفاتية: يم تنكرون على من يعلل الأحكام الجائزة بالعلل الجائزة، والأحكام الواجبة بالعلل الواجبة؟ فلا الاحتياج والافتقار غير موجب للتعليل، ولا الاستقلال، ولا الاستغناء مانع من التعليل؛ لأنا لسنا نعني بالتعليل الإيجاد والإبداع حتى يستدعيه الجواز والاحتياج ويمنعه الوجوب والاستغناء، لكنا نعني بالتعليل الاقتضاء العقلي والتلازم الحقيقي، بشرط أن يكون أحدهما ملتزما والآخر ملتزماً، والوجوب والجواز لا أثر لهما في منع الاقتضاء والتلازم، فلا عقلا يمتنع
That, and they deviated from this rule that He is willing, for since He was not necessary, He was caused. And all this is because the necessary is independent in its necessity from needing a cause, while the contingent, since it is not independent in itself—I mean one of the two sides of its contingency—needs a cause: either the choice of a chooser or the necessitation of a cause. So the existence of knowledge in the witnessed world, since it was contingent, needed the choice of a chooser to bring it into existence. And the establishment of the ruling of the cause in the witnessed, since it was contingent, needed a cause that necessitates it, namely knowledge. Is it not that the existence of the eternal, since it was necessary, was not caused, and the existence of the temporal, since it was contingent, was caused? The Sifatiyya said: Why do you criticize one who causes contingent rulings with contingent causes, and necessary rulings with necessary causes? For neither neediness and dependence necessitate causation, nor independence and self-sufficiency prevent causation; because we do not mean by causation bringing into existence and creation such that contingency and neediness would require it and necessity and self-sufficiency would prevent it. Rather, we mean by causation intellectual entailment and real correlation, on the condition that one of them is entailed and the other is entailing. And necessity and contingency have no effect in preventing entailment and correlation. So, rationally, it is not impossible.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Sifatiyya to a principle commonly held by the Mu'tazila or Ash'ari theologians (the author's own position). The passage begins with the author asserting that the Necessary Being does not need a cause, while contingent things do. He gives the example of knowledge in the witnessed world: since it is contingent, it requires a cause. The Sifatiyya then object by redefining causation (al-taʿlīl). They insist that they do not mean by causation 'bringing into existence and creation'—which would indeed require contingency—but rather 'intellectual entailment and real correlation' (al-iqtiḍāʾ al-ʿaqlī wa al-talāzum al-ḥaqīqī). On this definition, necessity does not prevent being caused, and contingency does not require it. They conclude that there is no rational impossibility in causing a necessary thing by a necessary cause.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wājib)
الجائز (al-jāʾiz)
التعليل (al-taʿlīl)
الاقتضاء العقلي (al-iqtiḍāʾ al-ʿaqlī)
التلازم (al-talāzum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sifatiyya believed that God's attributes are created by a cause.
Correction: They define causation as logical entailment, not creation.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, what do they mean by 'causation' (al-taʿlīl)?
A) Bringing into existence and creation
B) Intellectual entailment and real correlation
C) The attribute of the cause
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya redefine causation as intellectual entailment, thereby allowing necessary things to be 'caused' in a non-creational sense.

What would it take for you to expand your understanding as the Sifatiyya did?

But have you ever used this truth to distance yourself from God—thinking He is too transcendent to be involved in your daily affairs? This lesson shows that logical entailment still connects necessary things. How can you use this to see God's attributes as intimately linked to your life, without compromising His independence? The Sifatiyya: Contingency Is Not Caused
The Sifatiyya argue contingency is not caused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sifatiyya argue that contingency itself is not caused and how they avoid infinite regress.

Core Idea
Contingency is not caused; it is simply attributed to the agent.

The Sifatiyya, a school within Islamic theology, maintain that contingent things do not require a cause for their contingency itself. Rather, the agent (God) simply specifies which possibility occurs. This lesson examines their reasoning and the threat of infinite regress if every contingent thing needed a cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the passage and find where they state that contingency is not caused. Note the phrase 'لا يعلل البتة'.
The passage begins with this claim.
2
Understand the attribution to the agent
See how they say contingency is attributed to the agent so that he specifies one of the two sides.
This shows contingency's dependence on the agent's choice.
3
Follow the regress argument
Trace the argument: if every contingent required a cause, and that cause also contingent, then infinite regress.
This refutes the necessity of a cause for contingency itself.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this avoids assigning causes to God's acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواز من هذا الوجه لا يعلل البتة، بل ينسب إلى الفاعل حتى يخصص بأحد طرفي الجواز، فهو إذا من حيث إنه جائز لا يعلل بعلة، ولو وجب تعليل كل جائز بعلة، وتلك العلة أيضا تعلل بعلة فيؤدي إلى ما لا نهاية له. ولست أقول: لا ينسب إلى فاعل، وأن نسبة الجائز في أن يوجد إلى فاعل ليس كنسبة العالمية في أن توجب إلى علم، فليفرق الفارق بين تعليل الأحكام بالعلل وبين تعلق المقدور بالقدرة، فإن بينهما بونا عظيما وأمدا بعيدا، فلا يجوز أن يقتبس حكم من الآخر، أولا أن يطرد حكم أحدهما في الثاني إلا أن يمنع مانع أصل التعليل ويرفع العلة والمعلول، ولا نقول بهما في الشاهد والغائب، وذلك كلام آخر. ومن نفى الحال وكونها صفة ثابتة في الأعيان لم يصح إثبات العلة والمعلول على أصله تحقيقا، فإنه لم يبق إلا عبارة أعيانا محضة أو وجه اعتبار عقلي، فلا معنى لكون العالم عالما على أصله إلا أنه شيء ما له علم.
And contingency from this aspect is not caused at all; rather, it is attributed to the agent so that he may specify one of the two sides of contingency. So, insofar as it is contingent, it is not caused by a cause. And if it were necessary to cause every contingent thing by a cause, and that cause also is caused by a cause, this would lead to infinite regress. And I do not say that it is not attributed to an agent, and that the relation of the contingent in its existence to an agent is not like the relation of being knowing in its necessitation to knowledge. So let the distinguisher distinguish between the causation of rulings by causes and the connection of the possible to power. For between them is a great difference and a vast distance. So it is not permissible to borrow a ruling from one for the other, nor to apply the ruling of one to the other, unless one rejects the very principle of causation and removes cause and effect. And we do not affirm them in the witnessed and the unseen, and that is another matter. And whoever denies the state and its being a fixed attribute in entities cannot correctly establish cause and effect according to his principle. For then nothing remains except a mere expression of entities or a manner of intellectual consideration. So according to his principle, the meaning of the world's being knowing is nothing other than that it is a thing that has knowledge.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents several key points from the Sifatiyya. First, they argue that contingency itself (al-jawāz) is not caused; rather, it is merely attributed to the agent who specifies one of the two possibilities. If every contingent thing required a cause, this would lead to an infinite regress. Second, they distinguish between two types of relation: the causation of judgments (aḥkām) by their causes, which is a necessary entailment, and the relation of the possible (al-maqdūr) to power (al-qudra), which is a relation of creation. These are fundamentally different, so one cannot use an analogy from one to the other. Third, they note that anyone who denies the theory of states (al-ḥāl) cannot truly affirm causation, since for them only entities exist, and thus the meaning of 'the world is knowing' reduces to a mere statement that it has knowledge. The Sifatiyya thus uphold the reality of causation in judgments while rejecting its extension to contingency itself, and they insist on a rigorous distinction between necessary entailment and contingent creation.

Key Terms
الجواز (al-jawāz)
الفاعل (al-fāʿil)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Sifatiyya deny all causation in the universe.
Correction: They only deny causation for contingency itself; they affirm causation for judgments and other aspects.

Checkpoint
What does the Sifatiyya say would happen if every contingent thing needed a cause?
A) It would prove God's existence
B) It would lead to infinite regress
C) It would make the contingent necessary
D) It would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Contingency itself is not caused; requiring a cause for every contingent would lead to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your choices are simply caused by circumstances, or do you acknowledge that you are the specifying agent? In what area of your life do you need to take ownership of your choices as a conscious agent?
?
If contingency itself is not caused, then the ultimate responsibility for your actions rests on you as an agent. How does that change how you view your daily decisions, especially the ones you make without thinking?
?
The Sifatiyya argue that requiring a cause for every contingent leads to infinite regress. Are there areas in your life where you keep looking for external 'causes' for your behavior instead of owning it? What would it look like to stop the regress and attribute your actions to your own will?

Distinguishing Causation of Judgments from Power's Connection
Distinguishing two types of relation: causation vs. power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction the Sifatiyya make between the causation of judgments and the connection of the possible to power, and why analogy between them is invalid.

Core Idea
Causation of judgments and power's connection are fundamentally different.

In this passage, the Sifatiyya clarify that not all relations are causal in the same sense. They contrast the necessary entailment between a judgment and its cause with the contingent relation between a possible thing and divine power. This distinction blocks the misuse of analogy in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Find where the author says the relation of the contingent to an agent is not like the relation of being knowing to knowledge.
This is the key analogy they reject.
2
Understand the two relations
One is causation of rulings (judgments) by causes, which is necessary entailment. The other is the connection of the possible to power, which is a relation of creation.
These are not analogous.
3
Note the implications
The author stresses the great difference and warns against borrowing rulings from one to the other.
4
Reflect on the rejection of analogy
Analogy is only valid if the underlying principle is the same, but here it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواز من هذا الوجه لا يعلل البتة، بل ينسب إلى الفاعل حتى يخصص بأحد طرفي الجواز، فهو إذا من حيث إنه جائز لا يعلل بعلة، ولو وجب تعليل كل جائز بعلة، وتلك العلة أيضا تعلل بعلة فيؤدي إلى ما لا نهاية له. ولست أقول: لا ينسب إلى فاعل، وأن نسبة الجائز في أن يوجد إلى فاعل ليس كنسبة العالمية في أن توجب إلى علم، فليفرق الفارق بين تعليل الأحكام بالعلل وبين تعلق المقدور بالقدرة، فإن بينهما بونا عظيما وأمدا بعيدا، فلا يجوز أن يقتبس حكم من الآخر، أولا أن يطرد حكم أحدهما في الثاني إلا أن يمنع مانع أصل التعليل ويرفع العلة والمعلول، ولا نقول بهما في الشاهد والغائب، وذلك كلام آخر. ومن نفى الحال وكونها صفة ثابتة في الأعيان لم يصح إثبات العلة والمعلول على أصله تحقيقا، فإنه لم يبق إلا عبارة أعيانا محضة أو وجه اعتبار عقلي، فلا معنى لكون العالم عالما على أصله إلا أنه شيء ما له علم.
And contingency from this aspect is not caused at all; rather, it is attributed to the agent so that he may specify one of the two sides of contingency. So, insofar as it is contingent, it is not caused by a cause. And if it were necessary to cause every contingent thing by a cause, and that cause also is caused by a cause, this would lead to infinite regress. And I do not say that it is not attributed to an agent, and that the relation of the contingent in its existence to an agent is not like the relation of being knowing in its necessitation to knowledge. So let the distinguisher distinguish between the causation of rulings by causes and the connection of the possible to power. For between them is a great difference and a vast distance. So it is not permissible to borrow a ruling from one for the other, nor to apply the ruling of one to the other, unless one rejects the very principle of causation and removes cause and effect. And we do not affirm them in the witnessed and the unseen, and that is another matter. And whoever denies the state and its being a fixed attribute in entities cannot correctly establish cause and effect according to his principle. For then nothing remains except a mere expression of entities or a manner of intellectual consideration. So according to his principle, the meaning of the world's being knowing is nothing other than that it is a thing that has knowledge.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents several key points from the Sifatiyya. First, they argue that contingency itself (al-jawāz) is not caused; rather, it is merely attributed to the agent who specifies one of the two possibilities. If every contingent thing required a cause, this would lead to an infinite regress. Second, they distinguish between two types of relation: the causation of judgments (aḥkām) by their causes, which is a necessary entailment, and the relation of the possible (al-maqdūr) to power (al-qudra), which is a relation of creation. These are fundamentally different, so one cannot use an analogy from one to the other. Third, they note that anyone who denies the theory of states (al-ḥāl) cannot truly affirm causation, since for them only entities exist, and thus the meaning of 'the world is knowing' reduces to a mere statement that it has knowledge. The Sifatiyya thus uphold the reality of causation in judgments while rejecting its extension to contingency itself, and they insist on a rigorous distinction between necessary entailment and contingent creation.

Key Terms
المقدور (al-maqdūr)
القدرة (al-qudra)
العلة والمعلول (al-ʿilla wa-l-maʿlūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, the relation of the contingent to an agent is NOT like which of the following?
A) The relation of cause to effect in physics
B) The relation of being knowing to knowledge
C) The relation of creator to creation
D) The relation of father to son

Key Takeaway
Do not equate the causation of judgments with the connection of possible things to divine power; they are fundamentally different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Write one situation in your life where you have assumed a necessary outcome based on your actions, and then repent from that assumption. Make a firm intention to submit to God's will without imposing logical necessity.
?
Think about a theological argument you have made or heard about God's justice—perhaps that God 'must' punish evil or 'must' reward good. In light of this lesson, how does conflating logical necessity with divine power distort your understanding of God? Commit to never again using the language of 'must' or 'necessary' when speaking about God's actions, and instead say 'God does as He wills.' Reflect on the warning: 'So it is not permissible to borrow a ruling from one for the other.'
?
In your prayers, do you sometimes feel that because you have performed certain acts of worship, God is obligated to accept them? Denying States Undermines Causation
Denial of states invalidates causation.

Without states, cause and effect become mere labels.

Refutation of Causal Analogy Between Witnessed and Absent
Shahrastani refutes causal analogy from witnessed to absent due to dissimilarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the causal analogy (qiyās al-ʿilla) from the witnessed world to God's attributes fails due to the lack of complete similarity between the judgments.

Core Idea
Causal analogy fails unless the two judgments are identical in every respect.

In this passage, Shahrastani tackles a common theological method: using cause-effect reasoning from what we observe (the witnessed) to deduce God's attributes (the absent). He argues that such analogy only works if the two cases are identical in every respect—but God's knowledge and human knowledge are fundamentally different. Let's see how he dismantles this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening where the author raises a counterfactual: if someone claims knowledge is the cause of knowing in the witnessed, they must accept co-extensiveness (ṭard) and co-exclusiveness (ʿaks).
Notice the hypothetical structure: 'if someone concedes...'.
2
Identify the opponent's answer
The opponent says ṭard and ʿaks only apply if the two judgments are identical in every respect. Note the author's clarification.
The key phrase is 'تماثل الحكمين من كل وجه'.
3
See the difference between divine and human attributes
The author explains that God's being-knowing and a human's being-knowing are not identical; they share only a name.
Consider the examples: infinite vs. finite knowledge.
4
Recognize the independence of divine proof
The author concludes that any proof for God's attributes must stand on its own, not rely on analogy with creation.
This is the main takeaway: 'rather, if evidence is established... it is independent'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس العلم يوجب حكماً زائداً على نفسه، فيقال أي شيء فاعله أهي مقتضية شيئًا أم غير مقتضية؟ وإن اقتضت أهي تقتضي نفسها أم غيرها؟ فإن اقتضت نفسها فذلك سفسطة، وإن اقتضت غيرها أفهو موجود أو حال أو جهة لا توصف بالوجود والعدم؟ والكلام على الأمرين ظاهر فقد تكلمنا على ذلك في مسألة الحال بما فيه مقنع ونقول: ها هنا إن سلم مسلم كون العلم علة العالمية في الشاهد، وألزم الطرد والعكس حتى إذا ثبت العلم وجب كون المحل عالما، وإذا ثبت كون المحل عالما لزم وجود العلم. أجاب: بأن الطرد والعكس شاهدًا وغائبًا إنما يلزم بعد تماثل الحكمين من كل وجه لا من وجه دون وجه والخصم ليس يسلم تماثل الحكمين - أعني: عالمية الباري تعالى وعالمية العبد بل لا تماثل بينهما إلا في اسم مجرد، وذلك أن العلمين إنما يتماثلان إذا تعلقا بمعلوم واحد والعالميتان كذلك، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن عالمية الغائب وعالمية الشاهد لا يتماثلان من كل وجه، بل هما مختلفان من كل وجه، فكيف يلزم الطرد والعكس والإلحاق والجمع، أليس لو ألزم طرد حكم للعالمية في الغائب من تعلقها بمعلومات لا تتناهي، وحكم القادرية في الغائب من صلاحية الإيجاد والتعلق بالمقدورات التي لا تتناهى إلى غاية حتى يحكم على ما في الشاهد بذلك لم يلزم، فلذلك احتياج العالمية في الشاهد إلى علة لا يستدعي طرده في الغائب، فإذا لا تعويل على الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب بطريق العلة والمعلول، بل إن قام دليل في الغائب على أنه عالم بعلم قادر بقدرة فذلك الدليل مستقل بنفسه غير محتاج إلى ملاحظة جانب الشاهد
And knowledge does not necessitate an additional judgment upon itself, so it is said: its agent—is it something that necessitates something or not? And if it necessitates, does it necessitate itself or something else? If it necessitates itself, that is sophistry; if it necessitates something else, is that thing existent, or a state (ḥāl), or an aspect (jiha) not described by existence and non-existence? The discussion of the two matters is clear, for we have spoken on that in the problem of the state (mas'ala al-ḥāl) with sufficient content. And we say: here, if someone concedes that knowledge is the cause (ʿilla) of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) in the witnessed (al-shāhid), and he obligates co-extensiveness (al-ṭard) and co-exclusiveness (al-ʿaks) such that when knowledge is established, the locus necessarily becomes knowing, and when the locus is established as knowing, the existence of knowledge is necessary. He answered: that co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness in the witnessed and the absent only apply after the two judgments are identical in every respect, not from one respect without another. And the opponent does not concede the identity of the two judgments—I mean: the being-knowing of the Exalted Creator and the being-knowing of the servant. Rather, there is no identity between them except in a mere name. That is because two instances of knowledge are only identical if they relate to a single known thing, and similarly two instances of being-knowing (al-ʿālimiyyān). And it is known without doubt that the being-knowing of the absent (al-ghā'ib) and the being-knowing of the witnessed are not identical in every respect; rather, they are different in every respect. So how can co-extensiveness, co-exclusiveness, analogy, and combination be required? Would it not be the case that if one required the judgment of being-knowing in the absent to be co-extensive—due to its relation to infinite known things—and the judgment of power (al-qādariyya) in the absent to be co-extensive—due to its fitness to create and relation to infinite potentialities—so as to judge by that what is in the witnessed, it would not follow. Therefore, the need of being-knowing in the witnessed for a cause does not require its co-extensiveness in the absent. So there is no reliance on combining the witnessed and the absent by way of cause and effect (al-ʿilla wa al-maʿlūl). Rather, if evidence is established in the absent that He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, then that evidence is independent in itself, not needing to consider the side of the witnessed.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the causal analogy (qiyās al-ʿilla) between the witnessed world (shāhid) and the absent divine realm (ghā'ib). The argument begins by stating that knowledge does not entail an additional judgment upon itself, raising a counterfactual objection: if someone claims that knowledge is the cause (ʿilla) of being knowing (al-ʿālimiyya) in the witnessed, they must also accept the principle of ṭard wa ʿaks—i.e., whenever the cause exists, the effect exists, and vice versa. The author then presents the opponent's answer: this principle only applies if the two judgments are identical in every respect. But the being-knowing of God (al-Bārī) and the being-knowing of a human servant are not identical; they share only a name. Two instances of knowledge are identical only if they relate to the same object, but God's knowledge encompasses infinite objects, while human knowledge is finite. Therefore, the cause-effect relationship from the witnessed cannot be transferred to the absent. The author concludes that any proof for God's attributes must be independent, not based on analogy with the created.

Key Terms
طرد
عكس
علة
معلول
شاهد
غائب
عالمية
تماثل الحكمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a cause-effect relationship holds in the witnessed, it must also hold in the absent.
Correction: The relationship only holds if the two judgments are identical in every respect, which is not the case between God and creation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the causal reasoning from the witnessed (shāhid) to the absent (ghā'ib) fail?
A) Because there is no cause in the absent.
B) Because the two judgments are not identical in every respect, only in name.
C) Because the opponent does not accept the principle of ṭard.
D) Because knowledge does not exist in the absent.

Key Takeaway
Causal analogy between witnessed and absent fails because the judgments are not identical in every respect; proof for God's attributes must be independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You often assume that your understanding of causality applies equally to God, as if He is just a larger version of a creator you know. Based on this lesson, what is the danger of making such an analogy? Are you willing to accept that God's attributes are fundamentally different from yours, even if it means you cannot fully grasp them? What would change in your worship if you truly believed that His knowledge is not like your knowledge?

This passage argues that the term 'knowing' is merely a shared name between you and God. Does this challenge your tendency to think of God's attributes in human terms? Essential Priority of Cause Over Effect
Shahrastani explains essential priority between cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of essential priority (al-taqaddum bi al-dhāt) between cause and effect and how it distinguishes cause from condition.

Core Idea
Cause is essentially prior to effect, even if they coexist in time.

Shahrastani addresses a foundational issue in causal theory: how to distinguish cause from condition when both can be temporally simultaneous. He argues that the cause possesses an essential priority over its effect, regardless of temporal coexistence. This passage lays out that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the terms 'cause', 'effect', 'condition', and 'essential priority'.
The passage is cut off at the end; focus on the argument presented.
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms 'علة', 'معلول', 'شرط', 'مشروط', 'تقدم بالذات', and 'تأخر بالذات'. Understand their meanings in context.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: How does essential priority differ from temporal priority? Why does the author say cause necessitates effect by essence? How does this help distinguish cause from condition?
The example about knowledge and the world illustrates essential priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن يلزم على كل من قال بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط سؤال التقدم والتأخر بالذات، وإن كانا متلازمين في الوجود فإن العلة إنما صارت مقتضية للحكم لاستحقاقها التقدم عليه بذاته، والمعلول إنما صار مقتضيا للعلة لاستحقاقه التأخر عنها بذاته، وبهذا أمكنك أن تقول: إنما صار العالم عالما لقيام العلم به، ولا يمكنك
But it is necessary for everyone who speaks of cause and effect and condition and conditioned to address the question of priority and posteriority by essence (al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur bi al-dhāt), even if the two are coexistent in existence. For the cause only becomes necessitating of the judgment because it deserves priority over it by its essence, and the effect only becomes necessitating of the cause because it deserves posteriority from it by its essence. By this it is possible for you to say: the world only became knowing because knowledge subsists in it, but it is not possible for you...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage addresses a necessary question for those who accept cause and effect and condition and conditioned: the issue of priority and posteriority by essence (al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur bi al-dhāt). The author argues that even if cause and effect coexist temporally (as when a condition and its conditioned are simultaneous), the cause is essentially prior to its effect. This essential priority is what makes the cause necessitate the effect. Conversely, the effect is essentially posterior to the cause. This distinction between cause and effect is what allows one to say, for example, that the world becomes knowing because knowledge subsists in it—the cause (knowledge) is essentially prior to the effect (being knowing). The passage is cut off, but it likely continues to argue that this essential priority does not apply to conditions, thus further distinguishing cause from condition.

Key Terms
تقدم بالذات
تأخر بالذات
علة
معلول
شرط
مشروط
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If cause and effect coexist temporally, then cause is not prior to effect.
Correction: The author clarifies that cause is prior by essence, not necessarily by time. Essential priority is independent of temporal coexistence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes a cause (ʿilla) from a condition (sharṭ)?
A) A cause is prior by essence, while a condition is only temporally prior.
B) A cause and effect are always temporally separate, while conditions are simultaneous.
C) A cause necessitates its effect by essential priority, while a condition does not have that essential priority.
D) Both are identical in their relation to the judgment.

Key Takeaway
Essential priority, not temporal sequence, distinguishes cause from condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is the Cause of all causes. Yet when you face a problem, do you rely more on your own efforts or on the Cause? In what specific situation today can you practice tawakkul by prioritizing the Cause over the effect?
?
The lesson teaches that cause is essentially prior to effect, even if they coexist. Think of a beloved attribute you desire, such as humility. What is its cause? How can you intentionally cultivate that cause today, rather than just wishing for the attribute?
?
The author states that the world is knowing only because knowledge subsists in it. If knowledge subsists in you, what effect does it produce? If not, what cause are you missing? Write down one action you will take this week to acquire or strengthen the cause, not just the effect.
