Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
Essential Priority: The Foundation of Cause and Condition
The author establishes essential priority as the criterion distinguishing cause from condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how essential priority (taqaddum bi al-dhat) distinguishes cause from condition, even when they coexist temporally.

Core Idea
Cause deserves essential priority over effect; condition does not.

The author begins by asserting a critical principle: anyone who uses causal or conditional language must accept a real priority in essence, not just in time. This priority is what allows us to say, for example, that knowledge makes a knower, not the reverse. In this lesson, we will explore this foundational idea and see it applied to three key examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on essential priority
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's claim that cause deserves priority in essence over effect, even if they coexist.
Focus on the phrase 'التقدم بالذات' (priority in essence).
2
Identify the three examples
The author gives examples: knowledge and the knower, ability and the act, and life as condition for knowledge. For each, note how the distinction relies on essential priority.
In each case, one term is prior in essence to the other.
3
Reflect on the universal principle
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for theology. If cause and effect were identical in essence, causal language would collapse. This principle underlies many arguments in kalam.
Consider how this relates to discussions of divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن يلزم على كل من قال بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط سؤال التقدم والتأخر بالذات، وإن كانا متلازمين في الوجود فإن العلة إنما صارت مقتضية للحكم لاستحقاقها التقدم عليه بذاته، والمعلول إنما صار مقتضيا للعلة لاستحقاقه التأخر عنها بذاته، وبهذا أمكنك أن تقول : إنما صار العالم عالما لقيام العلم به، ولا يمكنك أن تقول : إنما قام العلم به لكونه عالما، ولو كان حكمهما في الذات حكما واحدا لم يثبت هذا الفرق ويمثل هذا نفرق بين القدرة الحادثة والمقدور، فإن الاستطاعة وإن كانت مع الفعل وجودا إلا أنها قبل الفعل ذاتا واستحقاق وجود، ولهذا أمكنك أن تقول : حصل الفعل بالاستطاعة ولا يمكنك أن تقول : حصلت الاستطاعة بالفعل، وهذا قولنا في الشرط والمشروط، فإن المحل يجب أن يكون حيا حتى يقوم العلم به والقدرة، ولا يمكنك أن تقول : العلم والقدرة حتى يكون حيا وإلا فيرتفع التميز بين الشرط والمشروط. ولا تظنن أن هذا الفرق راجع إلى مجرد اللفظ، فإن التفرقة المذكورة قضية عقلية وراء اللفظ، ولا تظنن أيضًا أنه راجع إلى تقديرنا الوهمي؛ حيث يقدر استحقاق وجود لأحدهما دون الثاني، وافتقار وجود أحدهما بالثاني، فإن كل تقدير وهمي إذا لم يكن معقولاً أمكن تبديله بغيره من التقديرات، وهذا لا يمكن تبديله بل هذه قضية معقولة، فيلزم على ذلك أحد أمرين؛ إما أن يترك القول بالعلة والمعلول والشرط والمشروط رأسًا، ولا يطلق لفظ الإيجاب والاقتضاء على المعاني، بل يقال : معنى كون الشيء عالما قيام العلم به، ومعنى قيام العلم به اتصاف محله به من غير فرق، لكن يشتق له اسم من العلم فيقال عالم كما يشتق له اسم من الفعل فيقال : فاعل، وهذا أمر راجع إلى اللغة فحسب، فلا اقتضاء ولا إيجاب ولا علة ولا معلول، وهذا أهون الأمرين. وإما أن يقضي بسبق العلم على العالمية، وأن يكون الوجود بالعلم أولا وأولى منه بالعالمية، ثم يحكم بسبق الذات على الصفات، وأن يكون الوجود
But upon everyone who speaks of cause and effect and condition and conditioned, the question of priority and posteriority in essence is incumbent, even if they are concomitant in existence. For the cause only becomes necessitating of the judgment due to its deserving priority over it in essence, and the effect only becomes necessitating of the cause due to its deserving posteriority to it in essence. And by this you can say: 'The world only became knowledgeable because of knowledge subsisting in it,' and you cannot say: 'Knowledge only subsisted in it because it is knowledgeable.' And if their judgment in essence were one, this difference would not be established. And we distinguish similarly between the temporally originated power and the empowered act: for ability, although it is with the act in existence, is before the act in essence and in deserving existence. Therefore you can say: 'The act occurred through ability,' and you cannot say: 'Ability occurred through the act.' And this is our statement concerning condition and conditioned: the locus must be alive so that knowledge and power subsist in it, and you cannot say: 'Knowledge and power [subsist] so that it is alive,' otherwise the distinction between condition and conditioned would be nullified. And do not think that this difference is merely verbal; for the mentioned distinction is an intellectual judgment beyond the verbal. And do not also think that it is due to our imaginary estimation, where existence is estimated as deserved for one without the other, and the existence of one is dependent on the second. For every imaginary estimation, if not reasonable, can be replaced by another estimation, but this cannot be replaced; rather it is an intelligible judgment. Therefore one of two things is incumbent upon this: either to abandon entirely the talk of cause and effect and condition and conditioned, and not to use the words 'necessitation' and 'implication' for meanings, but rather to say: 'The meaning of a thing being knowledgeable is the subsistence of knowledge in it,' and 'The meaning of the subsistence of knowledge in it is the description of its locus by it without any difference,' but a name is derived for it from knowledge, so it is called 'knowing,' just as a name is derived for it from the act, so it is called 'agent.' And this is merely a matter of language: no necessitation, no implication, no cause, no effect. And this is the easier of the two things. Or to judge that knowledge precedes being knowledgeable, and that existence through knowledge is first and more prior than through being knowledgeable, then to judge that the essence precedes the attributes, and that existence [is through essence].
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the essential distinction between cause ('illa) and condition (shart). The author asserts that anyone who posits cause-effect or condition-conditioned relationships must accept that there is a priority in essence (taqaddum bi al-dhat) even if the two are temporally concomitant. For example, the cause deserves priority over its effect in essence, and the effect deserves posteriority. This is demonstrated by the fact that we can say 'the world became knowledgeable because knowledge subsists in it' but not the reverse; the world's being knowledgeable depends on knowledge, not vice versa. Similarly, ability (istita'a) is prior in essence to the act, even though they occur together in time. The same applies to condition and conditioned: the locus must be alive for knowledge to subsist, not the opposite. The author then warns that this distinction is neither merely verbal nor imaginary; it is a rational judgment (qadiyya 'aqliyya). He then presents two alternatives: either abandon causal language entirely and treat names like 'knowing' and 'agent' as purely derivative linguistic terms, which is easier; or accept that knowledge is prior to being knowledgeable, and essence is prior to attributes. The second alternative is left incomplete in the text, but it implies a metaphysical commitment to essential priority.

Key Terms
علة ('illa)
معلول (ma'lul)
شرط (shart)
مشروط (mashrut)
تقدم بالذات (taqaddum bi al-dhat)
تأخر بالذات (ta'akhkhur bi al-dhat)
قدرة (qudra)
مقدور (maqdur)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that cause and condition are identical because they can coexist temporally.
Correction: Essential priority shows cause is prior in essence, not just time; condition is not prior in the same way.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes cause from condition?
A) Cause always exists before the effect in time.
B) Cause deserves priority in essence over the effect, even if they coexist.
C) Condition is always imaginary while cause is real.
D) There is no real distinction; it is merely verbal.

Key Takeaway
Essential priority (taqaddum bi al-dhat) is the rational basis for distinguishing cause from condition, even when they are temporally concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does it mean for Allah to be essentially prior to everything you do? Have you truly given Him that priority in your intentions, or do you merely acknowledge it in time after the fact?
?
If ability is essentially before the act, how does that reshape your understanding of your own responsibility? Do you see your choices as flowing from a prior capacity granted by Allah, or do you take that capacity for granted?
?
When you think about your own life, what condition (like life itself) are you taking as a given, forgetting that it is essentially prior to everything you do? How can you live today with that awareness?
